A RADICAL REFORM,
“Oh, I ft el so Mondayish !” exclaims the housemother
at her breakfast table on the Monday morning. Although
the above absurd adjective is not to be found in the dic-
tionary, yet every woman well knows its meaning when
she looks forward to the teeming, steaming, boiling, toil-
ing, temper-trying, hateful washing day. The house is
thrown out of joint, other weekly duties are, as far as
possible, set aside, the children kept out of the way, a cold
dinner for the family, and a warm reception for anyone
who intrudes to waste time and to prolong the agony.
Happy, indeed, is the home where “washing day” is a
thing of the past. Every busy, hard-pressed mother
knows the wretchedness and gloom which the present
washing day brings down upon the household. In the
early morning all is prepared for the all important washer-
woman, timed to arrive for an early breakfast, and then
to attack the week or fortnight’s batch of soiled linen.
The good-natured husband readily excuses a hastily-pre-
pared breakfast, for he hopes it may betoken the speedy
ending of the dreadful day. The children are packed off
to school, and then begins in earnest the strife between
the housewife, anxious to get everything finished in good
time, and the professional laundress, equally anxious to
make the work last over two days. With the greatest
impartiality the laundress divides her attention between
the discussion of light refreshments and the very latest
gossip, the intervals of time being filled up with a gentle
application to her special functions
Very many of our valued customers to w hom we have
supplied labor-saving appliances in the past, do their own
washing. In every family, in our climate, frequent
changes of underclothing are necessary. Then there are
the house linen, the cotton dresses, white coats, and
outer garments; and all this is heaped up for the poor
house-mother to cleanse, perhaps with the assistance of a
daughter, if there is one old enough. It is not that the
husband, who also works hard, is unkind. Expenses are
many, economy is imperative, days of depression and of
trouble must be provided against. So the washing is to
be done, and the wife must do it; and after the imperfect
rest of a sultry night, as she gets ready the breakfast upon
a hot Monday morning, she feels for a while that her lot
is cheerless, that the apportionment of toil is beyond her
share and beyond her strength ; that somehow' a change
should be made. No wonder that fair young girls, after a
few' years of married life, become w'orn-lookmg, and that
they age faster than they ought. The husband is w illing
enough to cherish the good wife’s comeliness, and to make
her lot easy, but how' ? She doss not complain, only says
she feels always “tired ”
Now, as purveyors of household implements, w'e have
long seen our opportunity to enlarge our business by
introducing a thoroughly efficient Washing and Mangling
Machine. Both these services must be combined in the
one article. This we have most perfectly and assuredly
accomplished. The demon of the washing day is not
scotched but killed
We resolved to introduce nothing until absolutely certain
by observation, experiment, and test, that the implement
would be a success. There are hosts of Washing Machines.
The vast majority are childish fads, are injurious, or are
useless. We say and we prove that the Beale Patent
Washer will save in labour of w'ashingin proportion as our
Sewing Machines do in sewing. And in a higher degree,
for the washing by hands is so much the more laborious.
The Beale Patent Washer may be described as follows :
The vertical tub is very carefully made, with fluted sides.
It is stationary, only the water and the clothes being
agitated by an accurately shaped “dolly,” which is worked
by a fly-wheel. The clothes are simply dropped in, no
arrangement or packing being wanted. The special
reversing gear moves this dolly sw'iftly backwards and for-
wards, and a very smaM expenditure of strength suffices
to lash the water into a mass of foaming suds. In the
process the clothes are floated to and fro, up and dow r n ;
currents and cross-currents of water are formed, which
meet, and are forced through and through the fabric,
carrying away every particle of dirt and stain. The linen,
being merely suspended in the water, is not rubbed and
scrubbed as in hand-washing. Neither has anything to
submit to the hand-wringing process, which insensibly
rips, tears, and wears the clothes. Thus the most delicate
fabrics cannot be torn by our system of washing. Every-
one has noticed that their clothes seem to be as much
worn by washing as by use. This is caused by the fierce
rubbing they get upon washing boards, or by the hands.
With the Beale Patent Washing Machines this friction is
done away with. Therefore, the clothes will last far longer
than by hand-washing, however careful. When the wash-
ing is done, the tub should be wiped out, so that it be
always sweet and clean. There is no false bottom, and
nothing to accumulate greasy sediments.
When you buy a Machine of any kind,
have a good one.

Dr. Speer is a regular Graduated Physician, educated at
Harvard College, U.S. (for confirmation see New Zealand
Gazette, January 21st, 1886; also New South Wales
Gazette, April 17th, 1888). The doctor has devoted 14
years to the study of CHRONIC, NERVOUS, AND
SPECIAL DISEASES. Four of these were in the leading
hospitals of the United States, which enables him to treat
all complaints with excellent results. All applying to
him will receive his honest opinion of their complaints.
No experimenting ;no Mercury used. He will guarantee
a positive cure in every case he undertakes, or forfeit
£2OO. His reason for doing so is this—when there is no
organic disease, and nothing but an inorganic trouble, or,
in other words, a conglomeration of si'mptoms producing
a certain result, there is no reason why it cannot be re-
moved with the proper remedies, therefore he guarantees.
He wishes it distinctly understood that he does not claim
to perform impossibilities, or to have miraculous power
he claims only to be a skilled and successful physician
thoroughly informed in his speciality—CHßONlC and
SPECIAL DISEASES of MEN and WOMEN. People
residing at a distance can, by explaining their troubles in
detail be treated at home. The course of treatment will
not in any way interfere with their business. Examina-
tion and advice free to all. Charges moderate. H. J.
Spkee, M D., 81 York-street, Sydney. Post Office Box,

TREATISE ON NERVOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE,
SYMPTOMS, TREATMENT, AND CURE.
rpilE directions contained in this little treatise will be
found of immense benefit to the sufferer; and if these
directions are observed during the early stages of Nervous

self.
This admirable little treatise is specially written to
show a method of counteracting the evil effects of indis-
cretions in early life—overwork, irregularity in eating or
drinking, &c. It is the result of much study and long
and successful experience in the treatment of diseases,
and contains a fund of useful information to every suf-
ferer from the following complaints, viz. Nervous
Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Eyes, Pains in the
Back, Defective Memory, Loss of Will Power, Loss of
Energy, Spermatorrhoea, Bashfulness, Discontentedness,
Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart, Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.
All Should Send for this Little Treatise.

medicine, Dr. Fletcher will GUARANTEE TO CURE
ANY CASE OF NERVOUS or SEXUAL DEBILITY
with ONE COURSE OF MEDICINES LASTING FIVE
WEEKS, and will send with medicines A WRITTEN
GUARANTEE to cure same or return the monies paid.
Dr. Fletcher’s treatment is both simple and effective,
and within the reach of the poorest. It is unlike any
other, and with it he has cured over 25,000 patients, and
invites patients to investigate his statements by verifying

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
iuiii be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under : —
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. ( Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated,
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred.
U. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred .
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

Subscribers Claims on the Company.
1. —Have the services of the Company’s Solicitor free
for their defence at the Police Courts when summoned for
breaches of the by-laws of the M. r.C. and S.M.C., and his
special advice in connection with any such cases.
2. In addition to the above the Company will lend sub-
scribers money to pay their fines into Court, which they
can arrange to pay off by instalments with 5 p.c. interest.
3. Are entitled to send their vehicles to the Company’s
coachbuilders and have any damage, caused by accident,
made good, free to any amount not exceeding Five Pounds
Five Shillings (£5 65.) for each and every accident.
4. Have the attendance of the Company’s Veterinary
Surgeon on their horses, and free. If a horse
dies from natural causes the carcase will be removed ; if
killed by accident Five Pounds Five Shillings (£5 55.) will
be paid for same.
5. A Subscriber is allowed two horses and one vehicle.
Special arrangements made where Subscribers are using
more than two horses.
6. Have the attendance of the Company’s Doctor and
Medicines free. In case of death from natural causes the
deceased shall be buried by the Company ; if killed by
accident, Ten Pounds Ten Shillings (£lO 10s.) in addition
to funeral expenses.
7. Subscriber being a married man can, by paying Is.
6d. extra per month, have the doctor and medicine free
for his wife and children up to 14 years of age, free also.
8. All medicines ordered by Company’s Doctors obtained
free from Company’s Chemist.
Subscriptions. —Entrance Fee, 3s. Subscriptions, 2s. fid. per month, or £1 ss. per annum, pajable in
advance. It married and require the doctor for wife and children, Is. fid. per month extra.

rpHERE exists a crying need for
the medical profession to stay
the quackery which is so rampant
in these colonies Men having no
medical qualifications nor aptitude
for the treatment of diseases, still
advertise with impunity as doctors;
and guarantee to do what they know'
they are incapable of doing, and
thus deceive the public who are
caught by their specious and lying
advcitis -ments, as is amply testified
by reading the exposures in the
Daily Telegraph from August 3
to 11,1887.
A chief cause of this state of things
is that medical hie'ii from mistaken
notions of professional decorum,
and because they consider it ivfra
dig, refrain from entering the field
against these quicks. .so long as this prudery exists so long will this canker consume our youth. At the risk, how-
ever of professional opprobrium I have decided to throw aside this false delicacy, and avow my sympathy with, and
readiness to treat such affections, in the hope of saving the young of both sexes from the ruin of health and mind
which consulting these quacks ensures, not to mention the ruin of their pockets.
A patient writes to me : “ I am in such a miserable state I would pay you what I could afford for a lifetime, if
you could possibly be the means of letting m? know what health is, and perhaps saving me from suicide which seems
to be my only release if this appeal fails. Kindly and humanely do not allow it to be in vain, and you will never regret
it. I have been victimised by a person calling himself a .‘.doctor.’ My father (of course I did not let him know the
nature of my ailmeht) paid this person £7 75., all for nothing, for which he said he would guarantee a cure in three
months.” Now, if qualified medical men would advertise in such a way as to bring their names befd're these sufferers,
and convince them of their sympathy, cases like the above which unfortunately are of daily occurrence, would not
occur : groundless fears would not be excited, and where disease exists it would be safely and permanently cured. I
am a and highly qualified practitioner, and have for over 20 years devoted especial study to chronic, nervous
and special diseases of men and women, and 1 am therefore justified in guaranteeing a cure in all casesWienable to
treatment.
• ' Sufferers from impaired vitality, or nervousness, should read my book on “Nervous Debility,” free by post foi-
ls where they will find much useful information and directions for self-cure. Those wishing a farther insight should
read mv work on the “ Male Generative Function in Health and Disease,” free by post for 2s. fid.
Consultation by Latter ... £l.,
Dr. ALE X. PATERSON,
A M., M.D., F.R.C.8., Ed., M.R.C.S., Eire.,
157 ELIZABETH STREET, SYXJ3NTEY.

SATURDAY, APRIL 12, 1890.
The End of a Liaison.
The Single-tax owes the factitious import-
ance which lias been attached to it as a pos-
sible factor in the practical politics of New
South Wales, in a primary and less degree
co the fanatical enthusiasm of four men—
Foxall, Farrell, Cotton, and Meggy—
(we purposely eliminate Garland, M.P.,
whose zeal to that of the others is as ginger-
pop unto Mackay rum) —not one of whom,
if we bar Farrell’s admirable verses, had
previously done aught to Justify him in
emerging from comparative obscurity. In a
secondary and much greater degree that im-
portance was due to the Sydney Daily
Telegraph, which, though seldom heard of
outside the province of New South Wales—
and only then when The Bulletin takes the
trouble to allude to it—has been enabled, by
the defects of its rivals, to win a large cir-
culation within New South Wales boun-
daries. Why the Telegraph did anything
at all for the “cause” of Confiscation will
appear later on. Suffice it to say here that
the journal not only began to do a lot for it,
but has gone on doing it almost to the pre-
sent hour. By mere dint of assiduity, the
quartette of enthusiasts just named gained a
footing in the correspondence columns of the
country and metropolitan press ; but, in point
of superficial or lineal measurement, the
King-Street Pen’orth has done move for
the Georgites than all the other journals of
the Australias put together. Thousands of
its columns has it, in the aggregate, given up
to the professional and amateur advocacy of
the Single-tax. No blather of the fanatics
and the cranks of the cult so windy but could
find a vent between its “rules;” no verbose
and long-drawn-out attempt at reasoning by
its more intelligent units but what could
be accommodated with a place in its broad-
side. Not a number—scarcely, indeed, a
page—of its diurnal issues for the last eighteen
months, but what “The Single-Tax,” in
bold captions to letters and reports, forced
itself with all its economic abourdity upon
the eye and the brain of the journal’s
readers, till by sheer wcarisomeness of
iteration the words blurred the one and
dazed the other. Measured by the debased
standard of the advertising-scale, the pecu-
niary publicity thus given to the principles
of the Georgeites—which, it may be here
remarked, are sometimes very different from
the principles of George— was worth thou-
sands of pounds to the “cause.” The
“cause,” in fact, had it needed to pay for one-
hundredth part of the amount of advertising
the Telegraph gratuitously gave it, would
have gone financially, and mentally and
morally “broke.” And yet this free, gratis
and for nuffin’ publicity was the least of the
things which our contemporary did for the
Confiscators.
A daily journal may devote a huge amount
of its space to correspondence reflecting
opinions of a more or less pronounced char-
acter, and yet be unsympathetic : nay,
indeed, be antagonistic to the source
whence they emanate. As a matter of fact,
and an easily verifiable detail of news-
paper policy, no sanely-conducted paper ever
does this thing, for such a paper, though
it may give moderate publicity to discor-
dant views, does not exist for the purpose of
pushing forward any sort of convictions but its
own. It is true we are not writing of sanely-
conducted papers but of the Telegraph,
and what can be predicated for the former
has not necessarily any bearing on the latter.
This being so, it was open to the Telegraph
to hand over its columns to the Single-taxers,
and yet to claim that it was still uninfected
by the Single-tax taint. But when it did
the only thing remaining to be done for the
“cause;” when it hired pens to expatiate
on the moral beauties, the economic advan-
tages, and the practical blessings of the
Single-tax, it could not, surely, longer claim
exemption from the infection ? a journal
that pays for and publishes articles of a
lefinitely partisan and sectional nature, and
purposely designed to advocate and earn
acceptance for a partisan and sectional creed,
cannot escape the natural inferences of its
act by publishing the contributions in the
portion of the paper given up to miscel-
laneous matter. Even the Telegraph is
supposed to know some portion of its busi-
ness, and it must have recognised that to a
journal which fills its columns in this way,
and piles for the amusement of its readers a
Pelion of paid-for slush on an Ossa of more or
less honorary slobber, is fairly attachable the
suspicion that in so doing it is playing its
own game, and not that of a possible enemy
or a disguised opponent. When, therefore,
the Telegraph, as all the world presumes,
handed its cheques to John Farrell and to
P. R. Meggy, and to “Unit,” and to other
journalistic screechers of the Georgian song,
to discuss in its columns —and not merely
to discuss but to advocate —the “Moral
Philosophy of the Single-tax,” it was
doing something which it and its readers
knew it could not do unless it believed in
the Georgian theory. Or —there is a des-
picable alternative —unless the prompting
motive for this curious course was a desire
which the paper dare not avow —a desire
which was therefore dishonest ; such as (we
will presume) a desire to use the Single-
taxers as the lady of easily-assaulted virtue
uses the decoy-duck to draw the victim of
the prospective black-mailing to her lair.
From one motive or the other must the
Telegraph have acted when it published and
paid for these communiques of the cult.
Which—the sequel will show.
* * *
From the standpoint of Telegraphic
ethics, the paper has remained non-com-
mitted to the support of the Georgian doc-
trine. Its square miles of voluntary and its
acres of paid-for contributions might, and
did, delude the Single-tax mind into the
belief that the paper was in sympathy with
it. The cranks openly claimed it as their
“ organ,” and even the two or three cautious
members of the clique avowed that “ when
Henry' George comes, the Telegraph will
come, too.” And not the Confiscators alone
held this view : every straightforward reader
of the paper must have shared it. But had
the Telegraph been challenged upon the
point, it would have replied : —“ We have
not spoken. Editorially, we have been
silent. We have published thousands of
letters, it is true ; we have published scores
of contributed pro tax articles —this is also
true ; butreehave not spoken out in the leading
columns.” Technically, this would have been
correct. The one thing which remained for it
to do for the Single-taxers it had not done.
All else it had done, or would do, but not
this. At least it did not do it—quite. But
neither did it do the contrary. If it did not
send out the Single-tax creed, with the im-
primatur of a “great” journal to the Free-
trade Faithful, as a dogma of the Nation
is commended to the children of Mother
Church ; neither did it use its anathema.
No, it did not advocate the tax in its “ lead-
ing columns ” —quite. What it did do, how-
ever, was to print occasionally leading
articles, in which the term “Single-tax”
jostled “ unearned increment ” repeatedly,
and into which could be read with the
greatest of ease a declaration of belief in the
Georgian theory. Verbally and directly
committed to the Single-tax, the Telegraph
was not ; implicitly and indirectly, it cer-
tainly was. Right up to the coming of
Henry George this was the position of the
paper towards the Georgites. The coquette
would not confess that she had succumbed
to the seducer ; she had only gone so far as
to compromise her name.
# * *
With the coming of George, the Single-
taxers looked for the public “arrival of
the Telegraph. The ’Frisco Evangelist
came, but the “arrival” of the paper did
not synchronise with the “ official landing
at Man-o’-war steps of the Apostle of Con-
fiscation. Nevertheless, it was felt that the
event could not be long delayed. Did not
the journal lend the brassiness of its best
trumpets to bulge out the volume of the
welcoming salute? It did! “ The Econo-
mist of the day,” a “ humanitarian of the
noblest sympathies and purest virtues,” “a
writer of force as logical as passionate ”
were not these ‘ ‘ elegant derangements of
epithets ” editorially slung at George and
George’s awe-strucK and Ko-tooing wor-
shippers 1 They were ! Did not the
Oracle speak, and did not the morrows of
the orations witness the publication of in-
terminable reports, and hysterical editorial
criticism, and more wordy frothings of the
cranks 1 They did ! Still the revelation of
the paper’s full and complete conversion was
withheld. Of superlative eulogies on
George's work as an auxiliary sort of screw-
jack that is lifting up the semi-moribundity of
Freetrade from the low level where it was
being asphyxiated to a higher grade where
there was a chance for air and life, the
paper was more than liberal. It was pro-
digal. Of the word that could carry sub-
stantial comforo to the souls of the con-
fiscators, not a solitary syllable till .
But here we must ask our readers to give us
pause ! The recollection of our vision of
the exultant elation which seized the Single-
taxers on the morning of the 31st March,
when they observed the D.T. editorial
headline : “ The Taxation of Land ” is too
much for us ; we require time to recover.
* * #
Eureka ! Thalassa ! Vide ! Whoop !
Hurroo ! The joy of Archimedes, the rap-
tures of the Greeks, the triumph of a
Chcsar, the pure pleasure of the Chocktaw
scalp-hunter, the innocent gaiety of the
Donnybrook boy, sound feeble compared
with the intensity of expression of the
declaration, as largely anticipated as the
realisation of any Messianic hope—the
moment for which they had hungered, and
thirsted, and wor-ked, and spent their beer-
money and their pocket-money—had come.
For :
As to all that has been said against the the theory of
Mr. Henry George we may well leave that gentleman to
deal with his assailants in his own trenchant way.
faces them in the Telegraph leader. That
could only mean one thing—only that the
Telegraph had at last came body and soul
over to the “ Cause.” “ Those who are not
with us are against us,” had said George,
and now the Telegraph had ranged them-
selves against George’s opponents and
therefore on George’s side. The anxieties
and the doubts and the fears of 18 months
vanished with the rejoicing-shout with which
Single-taxers welcomed the utterance above
quoted. And then they proceeded to read
the rest of the article —only to discover when
they had forced their fad-fogged intellects to
appreciate the significance of other para-
graphs, and to evolve from tortuous phrase
and involved argument the carefully wrapped-
up meaning that they had been most un-
mitigatingly “had !” '1 he Telegraph had ex-
pressed itself at last on the Single-tax, but
it had done so as an enemy—nay, as worse
than enemy : as a treacherous friend—who
has sold the citadel to the foe !
# * *
The primary proposition of the Georgian
theory, which includes the basic principle,
is given in the italicised line of “Progress
and Poverty,” chap. 11., book YIII.—“ To
abolish all taxation save that upon land-
values." But the Telegraph’s declaration
approves of a land-cum-property-tax ! Pre-
mising that a land tax —mark, .not a tax on
land values, but a land-tax—
Is to be regarded as a most equitable and desirable
form of taxation, and especially so under the conditions
of New South Wales,
it concludes its summary of reasons in favour
of such a tax by the pregnant —and in view
of the Ministerial inspiration of which this
journal is avowedly the receiver—profoundly
significant assertion ;
Land should be taxed as part of the general taxation
which all property should be made to bear. !!!!!!
This, then, is the culmination of the Tele-
graph’s coquetry with the Single-tax ! It has
not succumbed to the Georgian tempter—not
that it has remained chaste ; it has done
infinitely worse —it has sold its virtue to the
land-monopolist. A land-tax, as defined and
understood by the mass of economists, is
denounced by George and the Georgites,
and for these reasons : It conflicts with their
bed-rock principle—the exemption of the
products of labour from taxation. Its
minimum force falls on city property, and
its maximum on country lands, while, accord-
ing to their contention, the reverse should
be the case. And its incidence is less upon
the economic rent of land than upon the profit
rent which accrues from improvements.
"What George contends for is not a land-tax
but a tax on the unimproved value of land.
To assume an identity of character between
the two modes is to argue the identity of the
moon and green cheese ; and to use ‘ ‘ Land-
tax ” and “ Single-tax” as convertible terms
—and the Telegraph does both these things
all through the article of the 31st March—
and for the paper to imply that it is friendly
to George while advocating “general taxa-
tion on all property ” —the abolition of which
method of revenue-raising is George’s aim—
is either to display a most lamentable, and,
in this connection, most culpable ignorance,
or to willingly parade a moral perversion as
an accepted truth. We give our contem-
porary a choice of the positions—it can
take which it pleases. For ourselves,
we reserve the right to point out that
there is evidence in the Telegraph
article of more than a mere error of
knowledge or a radical defect in the logical
faculty. We conceive it reveals something
very like a moral confusion, not to say a
studied duplicity. George had come and
had gone. He had done all the good he
could directly do for the Freetrade party ;
and therefore there was no reason why con-
siderations of the worth of his advocacy as a
“ fiscal authority ” should any longer prevent
an expression of the journal’s real views on
the land question. But the Georgeites re-
mained ; they could not be shunted alto-
gether, nor openly flouted or insulted,
because they form the Pure Merinos of the
Freetrade flock. Let, therefore, the editorial
“ We” reconcile the irreconcilable : claim to
be George’s friend, gush as to “the un-
earned increment’’- and all the while go for
a land-tax as part of a . scheme of general
property taxation. This the Telegraph did,
and though the achievement is scarcely what
ought to have been expected from a journal
which necessarily preaches its. high-toned
devotion to the ethics of an enlightened
journalism, and cants hebdomadally about
the purity of its democratic faith, it is still
characteristically Telegraphic.
# # *
The Single-taxers now realise that they
have been tricked and deceived. We are not
sorry for them. Had their aggregated
crania contained one gi’ain of common-sense
they would not have expected any other
result. What—the Telegraph go straight
for Confiscation ? What would become of
Chairman Carey’s mortgages ? of Director
Robert Sands’ land-boom money ? of
largest Shareholder Gorman’s enormous real-
property-sales commissions'? of Shareholder
Walter Hall’s Mount Morgan dividends?
Did the cranks ever dream that a journal of
which Bruce Smith, of the British Liberty
and Property Defence League, is a reputed,
and Land-Auctioneer Mills, a certain stock-
holder, would ever yell boldly for Plunder
by Act of Parliament, and shriek for legis-
lative sanction to grasp it and smash at one
swoop both the major source of its adver-
tising income—land-sale “ads.”—and the
chief element of its subscription revenue —
the pennies of Bill Jones and Tom Smith,
who have sunk a life-time’s hard-earned
savings in a suburban allotment ? If they
did they are crankier than even we con-
sidered them. The Single taxers merit
no sympathy. They may be clothed
with the ashes of humiliation and in
the sackcloth of despair, and the popular
verdict is “Serve ’em right.” They sought
to use the Telegraph, and it has used them.
They had striven by its agency to force the
hand of the “great Freetrade party,” and
to make it play the legitimate drama of
absolute Freetrade instead of the composite
farce of revenue-taiiftism —they have their
reward. Their Apostle and Master was em-
ployed as a call-boy to his particular abomi-
nations —the tariftists, and permitted, as an
especial privilege, to ring-up the curtain on
a repeated performance of that curious com-
pilation of broad humour and idiotic ano-
malies : “Freetrade as it is known in New
South Wales,” while they themselves were
enlisted in the ranks of the claque. The
Telegraph has “ had” them, but they need
not wail about their injured innocence.
They were “on the same lay” themselves,
but, as it has happened, the newspaper got
there first.
Unearned Increments.
Dear Bulletin, —Unearned increments attach to
all forms of human labour. Wherever there is
an earned increment there must be an unearned
one. Whatever power or faculty a civilised man
cmpk>3’s there come to him as rewards direct and
immediate benefits, and also indirect benefits
derived from his association with others in a
village, a city, or a country—in any field of labour.
Let a man start business in the early days of a
community. He manufactures aerated waters or
spirituous liquors, or makes boots or clothes, or
prints a newspaper. Whether he work with a
machine or with his hands an increase in the
number of his customers will be met with a less
proportional increase in the labour and the
materials used. One pattern will do for several
orders, and the larger the number of articles the
cheaper will be the material for each. There are
in these cases an earned and what is called an
unearned increment —the former the work of the
worker alone, the latter that of the worker and the
community.
# # #
A newspaper is established early in the life of a
city. Its outlook is to a portion of a 20,000 popu-
lation. The city possesses such natural advantages
as attract strangers, or its Government induces
settlement by systems of assisted or free immigra-
tion, and the city thus comes to have 200,000
residents. That newspaper has expanded to
answer the new demands, and a profit which
might have been £IOOO a year when the population
was 20,000 may become £20,000 or £30,000 when
the population is 200,000. Extra machinery,
extra manual, extra mental work must, of
course, be provided, but over and above the
value of these there is a residue of profit
which has been derived from the action of
Government or of the community generally. A
writer publishes a book when his world is young.
Only a portion of his community read, and his
anticipated profits are confined to the patronage
of these. But Government applies a principle of
compulsory education, and thereby indefinitely
extend the sphere of his profits and puts into his
pockets thousands instead of hundreds of pounds.
Association with others and the action of the
State, or Society and Government, have given to
the work of this man’s hands, or of his brain, a
reward over and above the reward for which he at
the time toiled, and which at the time he possibly
regarded as sufficient. Whatever piece of work a
man does, if that work be profitable to Society at
large, there comes from it a profit earned directly
by the individual, and which may be and
should be paid over at once, and a profit earned
indirectly by the individual and directly by the
community, which acts as his partner or fellow-
labourer. The individual and the community
have each a moral claim on this latter profit.
# * *
When Henry George says that land cliffei’s from
all other sources or objects of labour he states a
physical fact. But physical facts have no special
value in an economic discussion. Whatever man,
as a member of society, busies his hands upon, oi
his mind upon, as n*means to provide himself and
his family with food, is for the time being his tool
of trade. It may be a portion of land, it may be
a portion of water, it may be birds in the air, it
may be fish in the sea; it may be a singer s
voice, it may be an artist’s pencil, it may be a


writer’s pen. The work may gnarl the hands, or
sweat the brow, or weary the brain, or sadden
the heart ; but in the proper social economic
•sense it is work, only work, human work. The
poet who drives his imagination, the ploughman
who drives his plough, the drayman who drives
his dray, all face the same way. They answer to
the one name—Worker. That land differs in its
physical qualities from phantasies of the brain,
from emotions of the heart, does not properly
come into consideration when the worker’s title
to the direct and indirect reward of his toil is at
stake. The man who goes to the land is either
driven by necessity or induced by liking; the
man who goes to brain-work is similarly
acted on. That the man who goes to
the land goes to an object which he has
nut created is utterly foreign to the ques-
tion at issue. Whether he created it or did not
create it, he can only now (in a formed state of
society) become possessor of it on the same terms
as he can come to own a horse, a cart, a suit of
clothes, a pair of boots —he must give the economi-
cal equivalent for it. If he do not work the land
nothing comes to him but the indirect, or, as it is
called, unearned increment; if he do work it, just
as if he bought a horse and worked him, there will
come, or should come, present payment for his
labour and a share in the value which his partner,
Society, confers upon his investment.
* # *
The lever that shall lift the rvorld to better
ground will be controlled by the principle not that
land is a unique property, but that it is portion of
the great common property. It differs physically
from a machine, but not economically. In a
formed society the man who gives a thousand
pounds for an acre of land and he who gives a
thousand pounds for a machine are equally
entitled to the profits, present and prospective, of
their purchases. It is true that the machine will
wear out and the land will remain, but that is a
subject determining or influencing the initial
value of the purchase and not the issue of it.
Herbert Spencer’s mental difficulty as to one
man coming to own the habitable globe and
throwing everyone else over the side is the state-
ment of an extreme which does not count in
human affairs. It might as well be thrown in as
an argument that if private ownership of money
were allowed one man might come to own all
the money in the world and turn every house-
holder out for arrears of rent. Might not simi-
larly, if machinery be allowed, one man be con-
ceived to obtain possession of all machinery and
thus perform all the work of a country ? For one
man to perform all work would be socially as
disastrous as for one man to own all the land.
Instead, then, of beginning with the principle
that land is a special property, the principle that
it is a portion of common property, to be bought
and sold like other property, will lead to clearer
views.
* * #
For then we can limit the individual, in what-
ever direction a spirit of undue aggrandisement
drives him. By a sliding scale of taxation the
State can take from the subject a fair share of the
profits which her partnership with him produces,
leaving him also a share and the whole of his
directly-earned profits. This principle will work
over the whole sphere of human labour, whatever
the form of industry may be and whatever the
material it is expended upon. The way to results
on these lines is already paved with legislative
enactments. Land laws aie in existence which
tax estates and which admit compulsory expro-
priation when the needs of the State so require.
Taxes on income are in existence which make the
large receiver a large disburser, and which, when
the State requires it, may be used to compulsorily
expropriate income over a certain amount. With
this legislative machinery, known to our
generation and understood if not universally
favoured by it, the introduction of a
new classification of materials, based on the
fallacy of a physical difference, is a diver-
sion from the onward path. To oil that machinery,
to set it more swiftly going, to make it reach the
rich man, from whatever source he derives his
riche i, and to keep near him so that when the
volume of his income threatens to be a menace to
the St.ate all over a given amount shall be taxed
away, is the need of the present.
* * #
Sociology does not pivot on land or on any one
property in the universe. All property, earth and
sea and sky, light and darkness, wind and rain,
and bird and beast and fish, are its axis, but the
power which puts it in motion is the mental, the
spiritual, the bodily competition between man and
man, woman and woman, child and child. The
centripetal and centrifugal forces that drive the
race along are loves and hates, hopes and des-
pairs, hungers and satisfactions, the tyranny of
the few, the cowardice of the many, the actual
greed of some, the yearning for fullness of all.
The past distribution of land is but an agent of
evil; amended, it will be but one engine of good.
Society is a box of discords. George attempts to
screw up a wire and promises melody, harmony ;
but even if he succeed in screwing the wire up
properly, it will produce but one pure tone where
almost every note, from treble to bass, jars. And
he cannot screw it up properly. Land is no longer
a virgin. It has been wedded to system after
system, been enslaved, been freed, been sold, been
bought. Its early lords are no longer its sole
©wners. The Hodges have now their allotments,
and under the plighted troth of the community
the direct and indirect increment of their posses-
sions must be theirs while the direct and indirect
increments of any class of property flow to the
possessors of it.
E.L.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Defence of Royalty.
Mr. Frank Hill, attempting a mild defence of
Royalty in the Contemporary Review, says “the
great evil of the monarchy is the social flunkeyism
of which it is the centre, the abject snob-ism which
produces, the base servility which radiates from
>t m circles ever widening.” If Frank Hill were
to continue discussing his subject from now until
tlie Last Trump sounds he could find no satisfac-
tory reasons why a system productive of such evils
as he admits ought not to be calmly, quietly, and
immediately wiped from the face of the earth.
Lue would be worth living but for its precious
social “circles,” which degrade the character of
those who blindly accept them as the designs of
1 hpyiDKNOE, disgust enlightened Radicals, and
embitter the existence even of those wretched
snubs who scheme and fret to reach one circle
hearer to the Throne, There will always be ser-
V| hty and toadyism in the world until the
people who encourage servility and toadyism
II ?.. pemg due to their intellectual eminence,
oiheial position, or bank-account, are regenerated
o.V wholesome ridicule. But who can excuse the
If « a l. >e °l )l0 in giving life to the canker-worm
’ nmikey ism, which is Monarchy, in order that it
lua y ‘‘at its rotting way into the body politic?
Because Victoria Regina is the granddaughter
of a doddering old lunatic she is fed, housed, and
dressed in robes of State, specially prayed for by
that pious bulwark of flunkeyism, the Established
Church, and set up as a semi-sacred symbol of
Hingland’s Greatness. If she could be secluded
in a temple like a pagan goddess, then there were
little fault to find with the Queen as an institu-
tion. The passing of a great popular Parliamen-
tary Bill might be harmlessly celebrated by stick-
ing lighted tapers round the Queen and requesting
her to drink the Nation's health. Instead of this
the people allow her to be used as a standard of
false distinction, by which her “subjects” are
permitted to measure their social stature, Ho we
come to have men seeking nominal “honours”
from the Queen, when they would perhaps seek
the real respect of their fellows but for the Queen,
with her snobbery-creating powers, being there.
We have women, who might have sought distinc-
tion for their charity and kindness, content to be
remarkable for having attended a Queen’s Drawing-
Room. The Queen is the source of a myriad of
miserable social ambitions, which would die
with the destruction of Monarchy, and be
replaced by worthier aims. Pension off the
Queen, turn courts into factories of useful
things, and abolish the dangerous distinction
of titles (save only official titles which are
necessary in moderation—Governor or President
is sufficient official title without such muck as
“ Excellency ” being hitched on). Yes, get rid of
these demoralising influences upon manhood, and
then there will no longer be even an imaginary
glory in entertaining a pimply, knocked-out earl,
or a pudding-headed K.O.M.G. at one’s poor
“ but otherwise honest dinner-table.” Mr. Prank
Hill seems to forget that the abject snobbery
which monarchy produces, the social flunkeyism
and base servility of which, as he so innocently
says, it is the centre, are all that a constitu-
tionally-governed nation gets from the old
fetish. Monarchy has no compensating good
to set off against its great evil. That same
great evil is the one attribute of the system
which is traditionally regarded as a virtue. Has
it not became part of the official English religion
that the Queen possesses no fault except her in-
disposition to flounce around and be “good for
trade ? ” She would rank as an absolutely unim-
peachable Figure-Head of State if she caused more
money to be spent in flunkeyism and vainglory.
The fools make it a ground of complaint against
the Queen that she potters away her existence in
the completest privacy possible for her to enjoy,
and thus makes herself an eternal reminder to
them of their childish stupidity in thinking the
Queen is necessary to the State.
Dilke’s Withdrawal.
Australia is informed by cable, under date,
April 2, that:—
The offensive remarks with reference to Sir Henry
Parkes in the chapter relating to New South Wales in Sir
Charles Dilke’s “ Problems of Greater Britain ” are to
be eliminated from the new edition in deference to the
many protests received by the author and publishers.
In other words, Sir Charles Dilke is going to
saw off Parkes’ horns and glue on a halo. Here
are the “offensive ” remarks, as extracted from the
unbowdlerised edition of “Problems of Greater
Britain ” ;
Sir Henry Parkes, who has something of the aspect of
Mr. Punch’s Father Thames, but with a clean beard, is the
patriarch among colonial politicians. His career has been
described by his enemies as a closely knit tissue of suc-
cessful artifices, and it is characteristic of the man that a
number of copies of the publication in which that state-
ment was made having been purchased by a previous Ad-
ministration on coming into office he caused them to be
burnt at a bonfire at the Government Printing-office. .
[Dilke is now making a bonfire at the order of Parkes’
friends. —Ed. B.] He is not really popular, but only followed
or admired, which is a different thing ; and, while he has
few old friends, has many foes. He is capable of large
ideas, and is often the author of far-reaching pro-
posals, but is wanting in grasp of details. He is
in his element in a popular Assembly, reigning
and rejoicing in the storms of debate with marvellous
physical power. _ He has a tendency to be jealous of his
neighbours, and in a colony which, as a whole, is jealous
of Victoria, he is the person who is most jealous. Sir
Henry Parkes has retired from public life more often
than a popular actor from the stage, and the occasional
raffles of his effects have not lessened the number of his
political admirers. Sir Henry Parkes believes in himself
and that deep self-belief undoubtedly impresses many of
those about him, and makes them too believe. He is one
of those to whom age is useful. His years protect him
from the assaults of the young lions, and when he closes
his speeches by reference to the whiteness of beard and
hair, which has come upon him in the service of his
adopted country, the people are apt to go and vote for the
“ poor old man.” [We can hear him snivel now.— Ed. B ]
When Sir H. Parkes wrote in “ The Strong Man
Like a rock that breasts the sea,
Firm he stood, in front of foes;
To his friends a sheltering tree,
That in changeless beauty grows,
he may have been thinking of himself; but in person he
has been as little favoured by nature with good looks as
Socrates or Darwin. For all that, there is an assurance
of strength in the massy features, and a consciousness in
the eyes that their owner is not an ordinary man. The
fact is this, with all his faults and all his weakness, Sir
Henry Parkes is the only great political power in N.S.W.
His sympathy with the democratic ideas which are upper-
most in Australia, and his devotion to the colony and
broad grasp of affairs, give him a greater hold upon the
people than any other Australian public man. His debts,
his poetry, are powerless to sink him ; and as a man who
knows how to use, like so many chess-men, the sections
which take place ’mid parties in colonial politics, he is un-
doubtedly one of the ablest of politicians, in this respect
almost ranking with Sir John Macdonald.
Very likely the fact that other mixtures of truth
and offensiveness concerning the immortal Parkes
have not been “withdrawn in deference to pro-
tests received ” is because their circulation has
heretofore been practically restricted to Australia,
whereas by being measured up in Dilke’s book in
full view of the English public, Sir Henry Parkes
is wounded in his most vulnerable point—his love
of Hinglish distinction. Among the most enthu-
siastic and valued admirers of Sir Henry Parkes
and all his works is Mr. .John Haynes, M.P.,
who is so great and politically important a man
that he last week actually got a vote of confidence
fiom the cultured inhabitants of “Bungwall Flat
and Coolongoolook.” And yet this big potentate
actually wrote of Sir Henry aforesaid within the
last four years (when bis present god was out of
office); —
The policy of the Parkes Opposition is cheek, and its
consistency is that of sand. It is excessively a cheeky
thing to suppose that this country is about to be com-
mitted once more to the hands of Sir Henry Parkes.
This man s day is past. It would be dangerous to give
him the reins of Government again. The simple reason is,
that distinguished man is suffering from a galloping con-
sumption of the brain. Any man capable of the freaks
which have marked Sir Henry Parkes’ speeches and
actions, particularly during the past two years is cer-
tainly not a safe man. To intrust him with serious
political work would be to allot him duties beyond hia
mental powers. And that is what must be said of Sir
Henry Parkes. It is a painful fact, but a fact neverthe-
less. And as the dusk of evening is setting on the career
of Sir Henry, it is fitting to remark what an out-and-out
one it has been. Sir Henry Parkes was born with
half the ability of a statesman, and got the educa
tion of an alderman. His life was a mixture of both.
Over and over again he has told us that he was
the arcliitect of his own fortune. And over and over
again we have thought he ought to have prosecuted the
aforesaid architect. That architect began at the roof of
the edifice, and built downwards; and it has been eternal
patching ever since. Sir Henry Parkes says he began
life with a sixpence. We’re afraid he’ll leave off without
one. This can be said of Sir Henry Parkes he legislates
for Parkes. We don’t believe the cause of humanity ever
found a true response in his breast. On his cedar chair in
the old Government offices by the tig-tree he was a
Napoleonic snob. He affected the first consul, and was
only his butler. Ho was a perfect O.ESAR— in wax. An
emperor without a subject even to punish. He would
have reporters and officials crawl to his feet, and his great
feat seemed to be to distend his nostrils as he deigned to
speak to the officers of the people. This is a true picture
of the man in a few words. It is a reflex from a mirror.
Nature intended to make the original great; but. she
struck work when half through the job.
This is nasty, but in the main it is as true as
Gospel. Sir Charles Duke, in revising his
book, had better re-touch his estimate of the Aus-
tralian Gladstone in the light thrown upon the.
latter’s character by one of his greatest friends.
Mr. John Haynes, M.P., knows ever so much
more about Sir Henry Parkes than does Sir
Charles Dilke.

If the senior Bar continue to follow the example
set by Salomons, Q.C,, in his anti-Georgian
oration, times will be very bad with the clergy.
In other words, if the lawyers, not content with
acting on behalf of the Devil, begin to claim, like
Parkes and Henry George, an intimate and even
exclusive acquaintance with the will of Heaven,
white-chokers, surplices, and Geneva gowns,
hitherto accepted as the insignia of the only
genuine and authorised depositaries of the
Almighty’s confidence, will have to give way to
silk and horsehair in the work of justifying the
ways of God to man. However, we have little
doubt that so long as the Devil flourishes the
senior Bar will find it more profitable to hold
briefs for his children than to scoop the collec-
tion-plate. But if the junior and briefless Bar—
we grow pale at the thought of what would hap-
pen to the clergy then! It seems probable that
for some time to come the clergy and the apostles
of the Single-tax League will equally share the
privilege of speaking as the holders of power of
attorney from the Omnipotent. Will everybody
please leave God out of their political speeches ?
The blasphemous belief is growing day by day
that the power which rules the universe does not
depute either parsons, lawyers, or politicians to be
its mouthpieces any more than it deputes cats-
meat-men.

You must judge by internal evidence whether or
not this is the correct reply given by Chief Secre-
tary Deakin to the deputation on the Bella
Ferguson case
Chief Secretary’s Department,
Monday.
Gentlemen,
After due consideration, the Cabinet have decided that
the prerogative of mercy cannot be exercised in regard to
the prisoner Bella Ferguson, under 10 years’ sentence
for infanticide.
The Cabinet fully recognises the principle that the
father of the child ought to bear at least an equal share of
the punishment with the mother ; in fact more.
But the peculiarity in this case, is that it is not proved
there was any intervention of a man in the production of
the child. Had there been any man in the case he would
have received at least seven years of the sentence and the
woman three years. This, however, was a case of spon-
taneous production.
Our laws being made equally by women and men, of
course fair justice is done to both sexes. In every case,
where there is a man concerned, he deserves the penalty
equally with the woman.
I have the honour to be, gentlemen,
Yours obediently,
Alfred Deakin.
When Judge Williams sentenced five young
Melbourne men to nine years’ penal servitude
apiece for swearing that one Cutler wasn’t in
Little Bourke-street, Melbourne, on the night
when a certain Chinaman was kicked to death, a
great hoarse shout of satisfaction went up from
the local press. The newspapers congratulated
society upon the fact that Judge Williams had
“spoken out’ on this occasion, and orthodox re-
spectability regarded him as another Daniel come
to judgment—until four other young men,
charged with saying what the first five had
said, were tried and acquitted. Then the
possibility of Mr. Williams having opened his
mouth too widely began to dawn upon the dull
understanding of even the daily newspapers, and
the faith of orthodox respectability in “warning
examples ” took a change for the better. As time
went on those splendid Cutler Perjury Case sen-
tences acquired the reputation of being down-
right scandalous, because they didn’t seem to
fit in anyhow with the acquittal of the
four men now at large. Something must
be done, everybody could see. So the law, the
daily newspapers, and the people who stand by
those institutions in all time of their tribulation,
seem to have agreed together that the first lot of
prisoners were guilty, although the others were
not guilty, but that some of the guilt had got
rubbed off ; therefore the sentences of nine years
should be reduced to three years’ penal servitude.
In short, Judge Williams, the hero of 12 months
ago, now stands convicted of having ordered 30
years more of gaol than was right and fair to be
shared by five of his fellow-countrymen. This
illogical and scandalous compromise of the Cutler
ease will apparently be allowed to stand. It is a
disgraceful ending to a rascally scandal, in which
the stupid old law, the hot-headed judge, and the
criminal police—especially the police—have all
taken a hand. There is no doubt that the last four
men tried for perjury were acquitted because
Judge Williams had passed such a ridiculously
severe sentence on the first five, who, for then-
part, were only guilty of going beyond the truth
m order to save an innocent man whom the police
were moving heaven and earth to hang.

so notorious, that shifty and orawlsome bourgeois
print, fehe London Daily Telegraph, “hits off”
the late baby-show held in Sydney, with its cul-
minating battle-royal ’twixt impetuous mothers
and impartial judges. The D.T. opens with this
leader’’laconism : “In Australia, where every
British institution is copied with amusing
fidelity,” &c. Now there is a patriarchal patron-
age and tender tel era nee about that statement
which eharactciises the Daily Telegraph and
other “great organs ” when dealing with subjects
and doings “colonial,” It is a marvel, though,
that, while on the subject of babies, the arch-
humorist who “worked up” this article omitted
to state that the Australian babies themselves (let
alone their shows) were produced with an “amus-
ing fidelity ” to the pattern of the British baby.

institution of reproduction of species had been
“copied” with enthusiasm (and “amusing
fidelity ”) by the colonials, proving that wo were
not a proud or prejudiced people, but are willing
to adhere to, nor striving to improve upon those
“ institutions ” which have stood the test of time.
The Bulletin wrote about the jealousy of the
military authorities towards the volunteer# in
England, the dread by Premier Salisbury and
others lest the volunteers, a truly national force,
should become too powerful, wherefore they must
have inferior arms to the regulars. Here is an
evidence that the same kind of feeling exists in
Australia. The Victorian Government has been
worrying at the Admiral commanding for leave to
send Victorian Naval officers on board the Eng-
lish men-o’-war in Australian waters, so that they
may have a little sea practice. But no, that does
not chime in with the Policy. After resisting as
long as possible, grudging leave has been given for
two lieutenants to go on a cruise, but it is ex-
pressly provided that they are never to be en-
trusted with any important functions. What an
instinct underlies this, what an affection of the
Lion for the Kangaroo ! The Victorian Minister
of Defence announces that he feels quite humi-
liated _by the conditions imposed, and it seems
there is to be some sort of an appeal to the Queen.

This is how the Melbourne detectives go to work.
Detective Christie, of the Customs Department,
went in disguise to a meeting of book-fiends. He
was got up suitably in a Mephistophelean mous-
tache, black wig over his white hair, and spec-
tacles. _ Next day he rushed to the newspapers,
with his splendid achievement. One must con-
clude he did, because it appeared in the press.
Either it came from him, or from a book-fiend, in
which case the disguise can have been of no use.
But Christie’s glory did not attain the climax
till a newspaper published an engraving of him,
with and without the disguise, and a laudatory
notice. It praises Christie as the most active and
honest of detectives, and, of course, nobody would
dare to write the contrary, even if they fancied he
was not absolutely at the top of the tree in either of
those requirements. The absurd publicity in-
dulged in by Melbourne detectives should be the
subject of official action. They swank about in
public places and at public bars. Everyone knows
them. People say “There’s Detective So-AND-
So !” Their portraits are published in the news-
papers, accompanied by lists of exploits supplied
by themselves. Is this the spirit in which the
clever detectives of London, Paris, and New York
work? In most countries the detective derives
all his power from being unknown. The Mel-
bourne men might as well go about in full uniform.
A thousand cases of whisky were lately shipped
from London to Bombay, and what think you was
the invoice price? Ninepence a bottle—nine
shillings a dozen. At what distance does this
stuff kill in tropic India? Oh, the blessings of
“our” civilisation! “We” thrust opium on
China at the point of the bayonet (and Bible);
and if India didn’t take our whisky, we would
want to know the reason why—in a Christian
spirit. One may calculate there is just a thousand
comfortable deaths in those thousand cases, but
then we can pray over them! All good cases,
brethren !

Sir M. H. Davies, the Victorian Speaker, is
mildly hauled over the coals by the Banking and
Insurance Record for what is indeed a con-
summate piece of cool impudence. He bosses
three or four financial companies. Two he called
after the names of his own two little sons,
“ Arnold and Co.” and Gascoyne and Co.” The
stupid nonsense of this palled on the shareholders
and the names were changed. Well, at one of hi*
companies, the smug Davies (in the chair) warned
the shareholders against “plunging.” The Re-
cord says such warnings come with amusing
effect if they are made by men who have realised
vast fortunes by promoting land-booms which
have enriched the promoters at the cost of a host
of poor victims. Davies is the prince of boomers.
We don't know why there should always be an in-
gredient of piety in these things, but so it is. One
eye to the Soul, another to the Savings. The latter
is a real good eye. For the other a glass one
will do.

Australasia’s fattest man, Tom Jennings,
the well-known host of the Harvest Home
Inn, Hobart, died last week. For many years
he worked a ferry in Tasmania. Three good-
sized men could be put in the boat and
wouldn’t outweigh him. Yet the angels find
he has a soul light as a feather. Soulfully he
doesn’t weigh as much as a seidlitz-powder. Tom
Jennings, who must have been about 65 when he
died, was a Yorkshireman—a fine, good-hearted
old fellow to look upon, whose honest, cheery,
healthy face took you back to bracing English air.
and rosy English apples and milkmaids and home-
brewed beer with never a headache in it. No man,
whether Premier or drayman, who ever shook
hands with him in his clean, homely little pub.
but thought of him kindly for ever after
His Epitaph.
Here lies “ Big Tom,” all knew him well,
He ran the Harvest Home Hotel
At Hobart. Many years was he •
A sight for gods and men to see ;
Twice sixteen stone of flesh and bone
The worthy Thomas cal'ed his own,
And when he took his daily round
He seemed to shake the very ground.
But Tom his bulk did ne’er disgrace,
His heart was in its proper place ;
And when it ceased to beat at last,
The whisper “ Dead !” through Hobart paus’d.
And friends he’d left this world to roam
Knew Tom still lived in H:v. ve t Home.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, April 0, 1890.
My Dear Moorabinda,—
At a recent titled London wedding a novel idea was hit
on for the wedding party—no ladies being invited on the
bridegroom’s side. This rather startling novelty will save
a lot of heart-burning if it becomes the thing, since it is
one of the most blood-curdling sights at a wedding to see
the veiled antagonism of rival mammas. They are usually
both equally gorgeously attired mi velvets and nodding
plumes, and both are equally anxious to he “ principal
maternal head,” as it were, and it’s the fun of the world
to see them manoeuvring. But sometimes the bride-
groom’s female relatives—who generally manage to feel
and look aggrieved at the turn things are taking-don’t
trouble even to veil their nastiness. I was at a semi-
private wedding once when the bridegroom’s mother in-
sisted on t aking her son’s arm out of church, asserting
that she had a prior right to clutch his humerus, and
leaving the poor little bride to follow trembling with her
mother 1 And, apropos to weddings, it's the correct
thing to he dismal on these occasions as who should say
“ Poor martyr ! what a dreadful example !” even though
the martyr is about to exchange rags for ribbons and fried
chops for French cookery.
But, after all, female jealousy is the root, of all evil.
The big steamship companies know this. Why do they
not allow each captain to voyage with his wife? Is it
because they think Airs. Captain would make him so
comfortable as to divert him from his duties? No,
indeed ; the danger lies all in the direction of the female
passengers to whom ho is not. married. The reason the
captain’s wife is not allowed on board is that she would
insist on being the captainess, the social head of the ship,
and would not allow her alleged lord to make himself
commonly civil to the fair creatures who had paid their
passages. She would be the cause of snarling and discon-
tent among the thousand and one women who consider
that the gallant skipper should likewise march them up
and down the deck by the arm, instead of devoting his
whole attention to that proud cat of a wife. Conse-
quently, every living woman would arrive at her journey’s
end with a grievance against the company.
A big Sydney jewellery-firm ar - e keeping an anxious eye
on the matrimonial impulses of brother Rupert, whose
nuptials with Miss Horsfal are still en espagne. When the
happy day was fixed last year the firm expanded into a
set of silver-mounted beer-jugs, to be presented to the
bride and bridegroom, but unfortunately the ceremony
didn’t take place, and the beer-jugs remain—mute wit-
nesses in a general way to the foil} of being sure to-day
about what will happen to-morrow.
Those engaged maidens of some years' standing who'
view the approach of their Gussies natal day with hope-
less despair, knowing that they have set him up in slip-
pers, cap, cigar-case, and all the gaudy etceteras that go
to make life a dreary burden to tire engaged young man,
will, doubtless, jump for joy and canonise your Sappho
on the spot when they read that the very latest masculine
idea is to have a pair of suspenders to each divided gar-
ment. The reason is that when they are hung from the
wardrobe-hook their own weight keeps them from creas-
ing or bagging at the knees ; and, again plurality of sirs,
penders is advocated from a “ save-time” point of view,
since every pair of trousers differs in length, and with one
pair of braces a man must be hauling in and letting out
until he’s tired. In making this fad generally known 1
feel that, though acting the part of a friend to my own
sex, I am doing a gross injustice to the voting aggregate.
Previously a fiance had only to suffer under one pair I
of hlush-rose-hroidered braces that wouldn’t regulate,
but now every natal day will shadow forth another pair
with “ To Johnnie, with Tottie’s love,” in brilliant silk up
the back, until life will become a mocking horror to the
Romeo who goes a-swiniming in company.
A typical fine Easter Monday attendance thronged all
parts of Randwick race-course, on 7th inst., to see “good
old Carbine” waltz off with the Sydney Cup. In the
vice-regal enclosure—which was simply overflowing with
gilt-edged humanity—the Countess of Hopetoun was the
most gaped-at, since this was her first appearance before a
Sydney audience. She is a tall, sylphlike vision with
hollow cheeks and an infrequent smile, and she wore on
this occasion a neat tailor-built frock of fawn tweed in
small checks with vest and Directoire collars of ivory silk
—floral bonnet of small white flowers tied on with white
tulle strings. Lady Carington, who looks positively
round and rosy after her summer’s respite, wore that
Parisian gown of sky-blue chiffon, Directoire frills and
full sleeves over slate-blue silk designed in bronze, bonnet
in blue and white and blush roses-cream parasol and

The “ official ” opening of the N.S.W. Agricultural
Show eventuated at Moore Park, on 3rd inst., when Gov.
Carington, accompanied by brother Rupert and a “ dis,
tinguished” following, set the misery in motion from some
carpet-oovered planks in the central pavilion iu a few
“ well-chosen ” words. And then we all went on an ex-
ploring tour after the exhibits, which appeared to consist
mainly of butter and horseflesh and a strong rustic per-
fume that wafted gently from the pig-pens, the denizens
of which, though not “ numerically strong,” as the re-
porters say, amply made up for it personally. Quite a
number of nice people visited the show for the opening,
but as the day was hot and muggy there were no new rags
to fondly admire or object to—the most striking get-up,
worn by a tall dark woman, being a pale-grey cloth, tailor-
built toque of scarlet poppies and scarlet moire gamp )
but the whole turn-out was distinctly last season's. And,
of course, the country contingent were there in t heir awe-
inspiring hats and boots and figures taking the usual
open-mouthed deep-breathing interest in separators and
other agricultural things that one would imagine they
would fly shrieking from whenever they got Hie chance.
Some people ling their chains, however, and I shall never
forget the victory in the eye of a tall, bony, angular lady
in lasting boors and a hair-net, accompanied by a revised
edition of a daughter, as she paused before a gaudy
bottle of jam, presumably the woik of a deadly district
rival, and pointed triumphantly to a spot of effervescence
at the top !
In the “Silver Falls,” now cascading at Sydney Royal,
one little thing shows the sound human basis of the play.
That is the dignified refusal of Lola Hernandez to eat a
piece of Primrose’s wedding-cake. When that cold-blooded
bride pursues her with some of the gritty horror on a
soup-plate, Lola rises to the awfulness of her peril and
waves the suicidal dose aside, while the house watches
breathlessly expecting that she will at last eat some and
curl up and die with poetical justice in full blast. There
is one gown in the “ Silver Falls,” worn by Edith Blande
in the first act—a yellow brocade lit up by sage-green. If
the tints weren’t so crude it would be “artistic.” As it
stands it is merely a nightmare of cheese and celery.

crowd were very late, for the first time in their long and

pale-pink silk with high neck and long sleeves, relieved

in pink and green velvet. The Countess of Hopetonn's

A city financier, the story goes, made violent love, the
other day, to a coloured nurse-girl, which heathen, in her
blindness, “ went for him,” with the result that the better
part of his whiskers adhered to her ebony fingers, and he
has been sailing under a clean shave ever since.
One outcome of the disastrous Queensland floods is the
establishment iu Brisbane of Hie “Flood Relief Society.”
The idea is to beg old garments of any kind from friends
and distribute them to those poor people whose ward-
robes are non est. Everything would be lovely if the
donations didn’t run so to old corsets. At a recent dis-
tribution one table was covered with stays of every con-
ceivable colour and every degree of unwashed wear, and
as the distressed applicants were mostly men, the show
was very funny indeed. But the benevolent ladies and
the parsons went home quite happy all the same.
“Garrick” and “coachman” capes are becoming
rather many in Sydney now, and, as the cheap Jacks have
them strung up by dozens in their windows at a shocking
sacrifice, I suppose Wolloomooloo will get there as usual.
So far as warmth goes yon might as well trot out in a big
collar as in one of these coverings. They don’t hit the
waist at all, and the influenza-laden breeze playeth
gently round you in a way to gladden the heart
of the chemist round the corner. And “ every,
body ” cannot wear them either. A slim damsel looks
like a reversed hay-rake attired in one, and the fat, brief
lady—but I cannot think without shuddering of what she
would look like in a frilled top wrap. Only a tall or
tallish woman of good proportion can hope to look any
advance on a scarecrow in a coachman’s cape.
I was looking at some of the new hats in a Sydney shop
the other day, and my great, deliberate verdict is that
they are all very old friends indeed. All the milliners
assure me they are the “very latest,” and that nothing
else will be worn this season but flapdoodle arrangements
in felt or straw or velvet that buck up on one side and
sag down on the other, with aggressive feathers and a
wing or two and a knot of velvet, but I cannot help
harbouring a base suspicion that the woman who expends
money on these hip-hip hoorayish topknots will find her-
self sadly out of it when the season is really in. It’s a
golden rule—if money is an object with you, of course—
never to indulge in riotous head-gear at the break of the
season, for shopkeepers are but mortal, and do sometimes
label last winter’s shape “As now worn,” while they hold
the coming “ cadie” up their sleeve, as it were.
Attracted by the commencement of the season, Farm
Cove is now peppered with warships, whose commandahs
always have some weighty official reason to give for their
presence in Sydney when the waltz begins to turn and the
Lenten days are past, and the bookies’ shout of “ 10 to 1!’
which you don’t hear often—although I had £lOO to £5
against Mantilla for the Cup—floats down the autumn
blast. Then the officahs abound in glory gilded, and
begin the old round of ogle, spoon and flirt. That is, of
course, if they’ve got over the grippe which was rampant
when the Orlando left Melbourne.
Apropos to “ la grippe." There is more to be feared
from the blistering “ infalllhles ” forced on one by mis-
guided friends than from the trouble itself, I think. One
cure violently recommended to yours truly is, to blister
the back of your neck and plunge your pedestals in
scalding brine 1 Another is, to sniff salt up your raw
nostrils with a hot poultice round your diaphragm 1 While
a third—a preventive published in the S-M.H. —is, to dn
the “Block” with your nose smothered in Vaseline,
taking a camphor pill at stated intervals. Of course, the
“ Block ” isn’t the prescription. But I think I’d rather
sneeze ; wouldn’t you ?

struggling sea of heads—some in big hats and feathers
which torture couldn't get them to- remove—l managed to
dimly comprehend that Kate Bishop—convalescent but

yallery; and that Alice May, in the front row, affected

Fancy work is taking a decidedly bibulous turn nowa-
days, have you noticed ? We are accustomed to the
glorified gin-casket with its shoulders tied up with
ribbons, and the curacoa-bottle acting as “ancient tear-
jug” in a scrimmage of gold-paint and a beetle or two is
every day happening, but the very latest development of
the craze for alcoholic fancy-work is the champagne
pocket-tidy. This latest pocket aggravation is fashioned
out of the straw covers of fizz-bottles. You clip it as
smooth as your cheek, plait ribbon round the mouth to
make it hold, tie more ribbon round the middle, more in
loops and bows at the bottom, and with a piece of cord,
also beribboned, book on to the top to hang it up by, why
—there you arc. I must confess that the champagne-
tidy looks to me more like a birch-hroom with a head-
cold than a wall-'ornament, nevertheless it has its wor-
shippers.
The officers’ tent at South Head was on Good Friday
fairly besieged with visitors, Hie fair sex largely pre-
dominating. 1 a vain the gallant warriors hinted that they
weren’t “ settled yet,” the girls would not he denied and
declared they would excuse anything which was ex-
tremely kind, as they were uninvited, unexpected and
unwelcome guests. To show, however, that there was no
coolness, champagne and whisky flowed freely all day
long. A fisherman’s wife disturbed the neighbourhood at
sundown, declaring ihat she missed two of her best
roosters, and cast suspicious glances at the tents.
Age cannot wither nor custom stale Melbourne’s most
veteran masher who, despile his silver}’ locks and at-
tenuated, bowed “gentleman!} " figure, still imagines
himself a Dr. Cupid. The poor old hcv, since his arrival
in Melbourne with the first importation or Cork butterl
has seen eight governors sworn in. Unless he shuffles off
shortly he will soon outdo Rider Haggard’s “She”—
“Even in our ashes lived their wonted fires,” and this ex-
quisite Melbourne Club man may still be seen in the front
row of the opera stalls with the other bald-heads.

Quite a sizeable human aggregate assembled at Sydney
Temperance Hall,- on 27th ult., to sample a pot-pourri of
music and recitation inaugurated and carried out by girls
in aid of the Women’s College. As a rifle, your Sappho
shakes her moulded fists wrathful!} - at tabby troubles, but
this one wasn’t a bit sepulchral, and the fact that men
bristled in the audience atoned for their absence behind
the pretty fairy-lamp footlights. To say that organiser,
committee, secretary, stage-manager, and “star” were
united with cool Pooh-bah-ishness by Miss Mabel Portus
speaks libraries for the energy of that young lady, who
wore a very pretty pink gown— a/ac simile of that worn
by Mrs. Brough in “ Caste”— and refused several encores
for her really soothing recitations. A fashionable feature
was a banjo-duet by two young ladies in fancy dress.
The rest of the crowd must think out those compliments
of the deepest dye that space forbids your Sappho to
indulge in.

David Buchanan, politician and advocate,
is dead and buried. Of him more anon.
The National Protectionists of N.S.W.
need by no means be dismayed at the result of the
Manning and Hastings election, which ended in a
city man, Mr. Vivian, stockbroker and Foreign-
trader, being returned by a majority of about 110
votes over Mr. M’Kinnon. Unlike his opponent,
the successful candidate had long been aware of
the approaching vacancy, and Foreign-trade money
flowed like water on the occasion. The whole
Ministerial strength was concentrated on the con-
test ; one or two notorious Foreign-trade hangers-
on whose speciality is the underground engineer-
ing of religious dissension and who never lend
their services except for good red gold, were sent
up from Sydney. (A certain Sydney Single-taxer
was offered £3O by the Foreign-trade authorities
to go up and speak.) Thus has another non-
producer with a boiled shirt fastened his political
teeth into the political vitals of the .“ green ’
countrymen.
