The Gaiety..., Harry Rickards
Toole’s first night at Melbourne Princess’s
bristled with signs that another venerable British
institution is likely to establish a large personal
popularity in Australia. Although he faced the
strange music in a character for which he was
obviously too elderly, the audience—or at any rate
the stalls and dress-circle people—received him
with a warmth that seemed to increase as the
evening wore on. It is true that a number of
enthusiastic fledglings had turned up with the
settled purpose of roaring at Toole, because they
thought it was the right thing - , dontoherknow,
which accounted for many bursts of inopportune
delight. To an older and fatter brigade of ad-
mirers Toole brought “the thoughts of other
years ” —memories that would have endeared the
grey and gentle jester to them quite irrespective
of his present powers to please. But the many
who essayed to sit in judgment on Toole, as a
comedian, briefly summed him up to be “a nice
old chap. ’ Coming before the curtain, with one
leg of his plush breeches gone wrong, to speak the
inevitable speech, Toole made no attempt to con-
ceal the fact that he is a nice old chap. As likely
as not—for the tact of John Laurence T. is pro-
verbial—he purposely allowed the sexagenarian to
stand very much revealed at that particular mo-
ment. Anyway, he knocked them with his carefully
simple address, and every member of the audience
regarded him as a long-lost uncle on the spot.
Whether successive crowds will continue to
enthuse over a succession of farces, played in a
theatre where some of the actor’s quietest humour
gets lost, is still open to doubt. If none of the
coming pieces were stronger than “ Ohawles ” the
question would be open to grave doubt. There is
certainly a thought running through the three
thin acts of “Ohawles,” but this trifle by IT. J.
Byron contains less real fun than the concluding
farce of “ Ici on Parle Francais,” which the im-
ported company rattled through with a decision
born of long practice together. In “Ici” the
necessity for importing Toole’s staff became evi-
dent. Neither programme, however, included a
part for John Billington, but the boss’s first
lieutenant is to appear with the first of the weekly
changes on Saturday next,
* « *
If the hand of Time has touched Mr.
Toole rather hard of late, it has not injured the
actor in the main. Less active than he used to be,
he is also less fussy and assertive. Many Lon-
doners were wont to complain that Toole was too
obtrusive in his farces, and esteemed him more as
a comedian of the best school than as a stage
funny-man. But his method now-a-days is so soft
and kindly that the multiplicity of his farewell
banquets can be at once accounted for by those
who know him only in his professional capacity.
From under light tow wigs beams the gray and
gentle jester—this last expression being suffi-
ciently neat to deserve repetition. Those of
the assisting company who appeared on Satur-
day seemed not remarkable for anything
in particular, and none of them possess
historic interest with the exception of Miss Char-
lotte Saunders, an actress sure to make friends
when she gets a better show. She was the original
fat jockey-boy in “Flying Scud,” fated to fall in
love when he had to ride under six-stone two.
Miss Irene Vanbrugh, reported to be the daughter
of a bishop or something of that kind, has the re-
commendation of being a mild reminder of Miss
Lilian Gilmore when she talks, but otherwise she
doesn’t resemble that vanished angel at any point .
*m * *
But it grieves us deeply to have to say
that the g-enial Johnny Toole is indeed a fine
old crusted specimen of the London snob. An
upper servant at Windsor Castle, one grown grey
in the service of royalty, couldn’t have prattled
away with a more sublime unconsciousness of his
begroveiled condition than did Johnny T. to an
Adelaide reporter : “ Here’s a point for you,” says
the great man, “ I was going to keep it as a bomb
for myself to deliver on my opening night. Now,
put it as delicately as possible. His Royal High-
ness told me that he would very much like to come to
A ustralia, but the distance ■prevented him at pre-
sent.'' Having delivered himself of which miser-
able twaddle Toole leaned back in his chair and
was astonished to find that the reporter didn’t
burst into a flood of grateful tears. In default of the
anticipated emotion Toole then pointed a trembling
finger at Tummy’s picture on the cabin wall and
sobbed on his own account. Poor Johnny ! Many
years of acting before a little clique in a little
theatre, and a long succession of little suppers,
gilded by little speeches from little lordships, have
narrowed his mind until he can actually dream of
witching Australia with the news that a fat,
uninteresting human satire on the vaunted com-
mon-sense of England would very much like to
come, but he can’t. Toole, however, hadn’t been
talked to by Arthur Garner when he so innocently
flaunted his flunkeyism. In the future he will
keep it for Toorak and Potts’ Point, we guess, and
be made to understand that Tummy is not a name
to conjure with.in any theatre where the manhood
of Australia is fairly represented.

George Coppin, in company with the
veteran G. B. W. Lewis, was walking up Collins-
street, Melbourne, last Thursday afternoon, when
another youthful old ’un slapped him on the back
and addressed him by name. “ I haven’t the
pleasure,” began the Hon. George when the
stranger winked six times in rapid succession, and
said: “What, don’t you remember Johnny
Toole ?” Then Coppin did remember Johnny—
whom he hadn’t seen for 24 years, by the way—
and they all turned into the Oriental Hotel, and
drank to the good old times when they both used
to play Paul Pry, and when Madame Celeste
raged in broken English at the Adelphi, and
Toole was best known aa the hero of poor Paul
Bedford’s “I believe yer, me bo-o-y !” Where
are Bedford, Ben Webster, Buckstone, Sothern,
Compton, Chippendale, Charles Matthews, Phelps,
and a dozen others whose names were household
words when Coppin and Toole last met? —
“ All are departed ;
All, all are gone, the old familiar faces.”

At night Warner acted the ardous double part

Nor would the plea of rehearsal avail, because

The Williamson and Garner firm advertised
that “ a stupid clique of nobodies endeavoured, by
libellous statements, to injure the prospects of the
brilliant season of Mrs. Potter and Kyrle Bellew”
in Melbourne. A very ill-considered declaration
on the part of the firm, firstly because “nobodies ”
couldn’t possibly injure the prospects of any
season, and secondly, because it naturally suggests
an enquiry as to why somebody does not sue the
clique for defamation of character ? But probably
the firm was only poking borak at Melbourne,
seeing they congratulate themselves that the
“ success” was “attainedlegitimately and without
any of the advertising claptrap, gush, and twaddle
too often inflicted upon the public.” Surely the
managerial Garner did not intend that sentence
as a serious reflection upon a faithful—not to say
abjectly devoted—daily press? It was all a joke,
the point of which was sharpened by a letter
which Pnrves wrote to Mr. Garner re Toole’s
reception luncheon. Pnrves said : “You know
how much I looked forward to welcoming the
good old actor from ‘home.’ and how much I
should have liked to have differentiated him from
the sorry procession of tailor-clad women and
dressmaker-dad men, posturers and barbers’ blocks,
who come demanding a welcome they do not
deserve.” After reading which, The Bulletin
would like to know what the firm’s advt. meant,
and what the Q. C. was referring to ; also, whether
the libellous clique includes Purves ?

The tremendous ovation allotted to Wil-
liam Rignold on his first Australian appearance,
at Sydney Her Majesty’s, was highly compli-
mentary to the reputation _of both brothers, and
prophetic also of the big hit afterwards made by
the later arrival. “ Now-a-Days,” a sporting
drama whose plot, like that of “Flying Scud,”
turns on the abduction of a Derby favourite, un-
like most modern melodramas is not written round
its strong situations, but so constructed that these
seem to spring naturally from surroundings. It is
without doubt the strongest piece of stage-craft of
its kind that has lately come Sydney-wards, and
in making this statement we are backed up by
public opinion, for an enormous house lustily ap-
plauded every point and enthusiastically called
the leading performers before the curtain at the
close of each act. William Rignold, a man
several sizes larger than his brother, George, is a
very fine actor and well-suited with a part. His
performance of Jack Saxton, the giant Yorkshire-
man and coal-mine owner, was to us like the revival
of a lost art, for no new-comer of late years has
visited these shores with such a consistently good
provincial accent, with a personality so admirably
adapted to his impersonation, and such an un-
doubtedly faithful conception of the manner i n which
an unpolished, careful, yet good-hearted countryman
would conduct himself in varying circumstances.
As Sir Harry Croydon, H. R. Jewett has once
more to tackle the polished villain, and this time
he plays it for all the part is worth, and gets
soundly hissed for his pains. A. E. Greenaway’s
Tom Saxton is a well-balanced, carefully-thought-
out, and meritorious performance. Mr. Hazlitt,
as Gabriel Harper, agreeably surprised us; Joe
Tolano, as “Shandygaff,” a black-leg, is fitted to-
the life; as the “Baby,” his partner in crime,
J. W. Sweeney most effectually conceals his iden-
tity; Stirling Whyte has an admirable drunken
scene as Bookmaker Dowling; Frank Harcourt,
as the German waiter, makes a hit; and for the
first time, as Bob the Jockey, Frank Ayrton gives
an Australian audience a good idea of his private
identity. Walter Rivers and Guyton Heath, as
Jockeys, are to be highly commended —in fact,
William Rignold seems to have inspired his
confreres with a share of his own admirable
vigour and enthusiasm. Miss Bessie Rig-
uold’s performance is more remarkable for the
possibilities of future excellences displayed than
for their “ Now-a-Days ” manifestation ; as Jenny
Dowling, Roland Watts-Phillips was clear in
enunciation and more than equal to every emer-
gency ; Madge Seymour, as Kitty, takes another
step forward in public estimation ; and 3lay Ellis,
in a small part, showed signs of future successes
as a soubrette. “Now-a-days ” might have been
much better staged ; some of the scenery we have
seen before, and the back-cloth in the: race-scene
was either so skimpy or so ill-covered by wings
that its “ prompt ” edge could be easily seen from
the O.P. side of the circle. Nevertheless, it will
be a very big “go.”

The good old “Private Secretary” bobbed
up serenely on Faster Monday at the renovated
Sydney Gaiety, in the presence of a large and
appreciative audience. Messrs. Tubbs and Biggs
met with a good reception, but Sydney folks seem
to have almost forgotten that sterling actress,
Annie Taylor, once a great favourite. Frank
Thornton has apparently added some new phases
of facial misery to his performance, and, as his
allusions to “crodio influedza” have now a point
that they never had before, ho takes the audience
with him from start to finish, just as surely as he
does his go-loshes. H. R. Harwood’s irritability
as Catterrnolo, like Thornton’s agility in settee-
clearing, is not so marked as of yore, but he has
got several new “ gags, ” and therefore gets there
just the same. The performance of Frank Cates,
as Cattermole the younger, has all the clearness,
the correctness of a stereo.: we failed to see a new
point in it. “ Chai'ley ” Brown’s “ Tailor Gibson ”
is as good as ever, Eille Norwood’s nervous huski-
ness deprived him somewhat of the big success
that would have been made otherwise by his
“Bird Song,” and I’. 0. Corlesse is bearable as
Mr. MJr si and. As for the ladies, Annie Taylor’s
Miss Ashford is up.to previous sample; Ada Lee
gets there with both feet as Edith Maryland, and
the Misses Brenton -and Gibson are even less
paralysingly successful than we anticipated. Our
old friend “ Kitts ” is in front, and a big six weeks
is seemingly in store for the little theatre.
* * *
With his perennial pertinacity. Frank M.
Clark returns hither, and dumps down a well-
assorted load of performers in our midst. The
latest importation, named the Boston Ideal Com-
pany, was submitted for public approval at the
Opera House on Saturday evening last. The first
parcel to hand consisted of Messrs. Burns and
Donnelly, who were pervaded by a strong Hiber-
nian flavour. Burns carries a face about with
him that is a cross between M‘Ginty’s and an
indiarubber shoe, and he works it to laughable
advantage. With several apologies to Alfred
Bennett for infringement of copyright, we may
say that their act “took well, and gave great
pleasure to all.” The clever colonial comedian,
Will Whitburn, has been sandwiched between
Boston and Australia, and contributes some items
with success,, including a song, written and com-
posed by Lance Lenton, entitled the “ Larrikin’s
Hop.” Miss May Yohe, who owns an excellent
contralto voice, diversified matters with an
Amurrkan valse song, albeit she was dressed in
Oriental costume. Franks and Marion made
things boom along in their specialty, intermixed
with acrobatic dancing. Then came Ellwood, who
is a male soprano in petticoats and bustle to match.
Many in the audience were puzzled as to his, her, or
its sex, but the performer eventually solved the
conundrum by emitting a couple of bass whoops
and doffing his head-gear. “Fat” M. Clark
next hove in sight, supporting a blonde wig and
a brunette moustache, and allowed three songs to
escape from his bronchial tubes to the delight of
the audience. After his retirement the Tudors
reigned, thus upsetting the hitherto historical
fable that the House of Tudor ended with the
decease of Queen Elizabeth. They ran a tinpot
band and indulged in a sword combat while flog-
ging the stage with clogs. The curtain finally
fell upon a really funny farce, “ The Foiled
Mashers,” in which Burns and Franks, as an
Irishman and a German respectively, convulsed
the house with their vagaries. Taken throughout,
the Boston Ideal Show is a good one.

11l the second act of “ Silver Falls
at Sydney Royal there is a beautiful scene
by Brunton of distant hills, lightly veiled by
a haze of summer heat, rising from behind a
fine, bold foreground of rocks that is per-
haps too highly tinted in the middle distance.
E. B. Russell, ” whose Jacob M'Closky was the
chief feature of the “ Octoroon,” makes the most
of a small part, as does C. B. Westmacott, also an
“Octoroon” success, with Sheriff Dixon. In
Bob Maguire, G. R. Melville has about the only
loveable male character in the play, and he fills
the bill. Clara Cowper improves very much on
acquaintance, Pollie Emery’s brogue clings to her
more tightly than on the first night, and the little
woman is now quite a favourite. Assisted by the
holidays, the “ Silver Falls ” will just struggle
through its allotted period.
* * *
“ The Sultan of Mocha,” at Sydney
Criterion, has hit the public taste. The Pen-
sioner’s Chorus is now in high favour, and the
dancing of Tilly Lake and her mate in the sailor’s
hornpipe, is much admired, though the general
opinion is that the ballet, no matter how attired,
is “ a bit off” on the light fantastic. The princi-
pals continue in good form, and “Mocha” will
probably be served up for another fortnight.
** * *
Since John F. Sheridan, the only one and
indivisible Widow O’Brien, first fluttered in this
part of the globe with that vehicle for variety
vagaries styled “ Fun on the Bristol,” several
imitators have sprung into ephemeral existence.
Sheridan, as the amorous Widow, is as sparkling
as champagne, but “John F.” Williams, with
his servile imitation, is as dull as a long-
drawn Woolloomooloo beer. Frank Smith
has certainly staged the piece well at Sydney
Standard, and the redeeming features in the
representation are personified in Martin Hagan,
Arthur Farley, F. Walton and Perry Ryan.
Among the weaker sex Vera Patey and Lulu
Ryan occasionally coruscate. A weighty hank
of olio business spreads itself widely over the
second act of this long-opened histrionic oyster,
and a good helping of marmalade fails on the
platters of the Sisters Blair for their vocal and
terpsichorean duettising. The Leslie Bros., about
the best in their line ever seen or heard here,
are no “fakers,” but play in a musicianly
manner on many instruments.
** * *
The benefit of G. C. Mila at Sydney Royal
last Saturday was financially a failure, chiefly
begguse the day (Cup-day) was ill-chosen. Artisti-
cally, the violin solos of Mons. Poussard was the
most successful item on the programme, and next
in merit to our mind came the charming perform-
ance of Clara Cowper as the young girl in “Darby
and Joan.” Most of the Royal company with-
drew from the bill, annoyed by the manner in
which they were lumped together, while one of
their number was “starred ” in aggressively large
capitals. The Forum scene from “Julius Caesar” 1
was creditably staged and well worked-up by Mr.
Miln’s company, considering the short space of
time available for preparation, but his reading of
Marc Antony’s oration was not by any means a
good one, and H. N, Douglas’ innovation in
reading his speech from a scroll, as Brutus, seemed
to us purposeless and clumsy.

Her Majesty's (Sydney ) was crammed to
the dour on Good Friday night, when Hazon's
orchestra, Frances Savilie, Mrs. Vauderveer-
Green, William Walshe, and “Daddy” Halle-
well gave a high-class entertainment. We think
that the “March of the Israelites,” from Costa’s
“Eli,”, was the event of the evening, but the
overture to the “ I Promessi Sposi” of Ponchielli
received quite an ovation ; Schumann’s exquisite
“ Traumerei ” had to be repeated, and the “Funeral
March of Ophelia” (Facoio) was magnificently
played. The tender and delicate “ Ave Maria,”
from Verdi’s “Otellu,” beautifully rendered by
France's Savilie, was encored, and the vocal quar-
tette, “ God is a Spirit,” from Sterndale Bennett’s
“Woman of Samaria,” deserved and received a
similar compliment. The concert was managed
by Mr. Churchill-Otton, a gentleman who, unlike
most managers, does not obtrude himself on public
notice.

Darlinghurst Skating Rink, with a replaned
floor, soft coloured lights and completely refitted
smoking and retiring rooms, is now in full swing.
The instructors are all attired in uniform, the
skate-boys in sailor-costume and the band is some-
what above the average, so that Manager Davis
may not unreasonably expect to do a phenomenally
big business, especially as the opposition seems
likely to be limited.

At Bondi Aquarium, “Billy ” Warner, the
inimitable; Silvo, the juggler and equilibrist; the
Silver Brothers, gymnasts; Jackson, the wire-
walker; Ethel Shields, the slack-wire child per-
former ; the Sisters Blair, the dance and songists;

entertainers are on show for a time.
& &- * *
Messrs. Brough and Boucicault’s new
Bijou, a delightful temple of art that seems to be
nearer Heaven than it was, and is led up to by
several festive flights of marble steps, received a
noble inauguration on Saturday night. Paunchy
legislators, wreathed in condescending smiles,
were scattered about in reckless profusion, feasting
their beery eyes upon a scene which will ever Lve
in the memories of such as had booked an elegant
plush-seat beforehand. Briefly, the Bijou is a
smart, roomy, brilliantly-lighted house, situated
on a gentle slope, so that the sweet maiden
with a preposterous hat on her fair young
brow doesn’t induce the man behind to pray
that she will fall a lifeless corpse every time
he wishes to discover what it’s all about.
Mr. Spong’s drop-curtain is a radiant item in
the general joy, and the comfort of patrons has
been studied, etcetera, by the enterprising thin-
gummy. With all these things in its favour,
“ Money ” is not likely to result in a long-sustained
rush to the new theatre. Even Messrs. Brough
and Boucicault’s clever company can show no good
reason for reviving a worn-out aphorism that
walks on stilts through five acts. The character-
sketches with which the padded author pleased a
past generation seemed very commonplace to-day,
while the chief character was always a hero who
wanted kicking, like his artistic relative,
Claude Melnotte. An opinion that all the
old favourites have played to better advan-
tage was generally expressed, and as their
merits have been acknowledged in The Bulletin
every week throughout a long season in Sydney, it
is only necessary to record the fact that their Mel-
bourne reappearance in “Money” was not the
immense success it might have been. Mr. Percy
Lyndal fell especially flat as Alfred Evelyn, a
mawkish prig who can only be made endurable by
a vigorous leading juvenile with a florid gushing
style. Mr. Lyndal, in this unsuitable character,
suggests a chemist or a curate. Mr. J. E. Cath-
cart, as Sir John Yesey, is too fond of taking
three tragedy strides after he Iras tried to make a
point at the wrong place, and, take it altogether,
“ Money ” requires all the popularity of B. and 8.,
and the novelty of its surroundings, to keep the
show going.

Meanwhile “Paul Jones ” continues to cut
the record at Melbourne Opera House. It is
stated and believed—a most mrusual conjunction
of circumstance! —that the first six nights pro-
duced £lBOO, so the quality of the notes that come
from Nellie Stewart’s chest grows richer every
night. She seldom sings less than a “tenner”
now, but Woodfield, somehow, fails to get off as
much tenor as is expected of him. Au contrairey,
as the Chinese say, Aggie Kelton has picked up
enough of her voice to get an encore for “ Look at
my Sabots! ” a delicious song and chorus
which might be changed into “I am such an
artless thing ” if they want to show where
Plauquette struck his idea. Madame Burton,
Snazelle, Leitch, and everybody else improve on
acquaintance, like square-gin. As for the chorus,
they bang creation altogether. They never wait
to be introduced to notice by the plot, but just
tear on when the heart sighs for ’em and scoot
before they have made even the men tired. Now,
fellers grow weary of surveying forests of legs
much quicker than their female relations do.
Mrs. Dampier and Miss Lily returned to
their places in ‘ ‘ Robbery Under Arms ” on Satur-
day, whereby Melb. Alexandra was stimulated to
wilder enthusiasm than usual. The Cogills broke
out into a fresh atrocity, called “Drunk,” at St.
George’s Hall; General Mite staggered bucolic
pilgrims at the Waxworks, and the Oyclorama
was having a holiday time of it.
* * #
The new Gaiety, a hall that seems far
more of a theatre than most of its fusty rivals in
Melbourne, absorbed some ten or twelve hundred
followers of the immortal Harry Rickards on
Saturday. The Rickards show is certain to
flourish here for a large quantity of reasons, one
of them being that the accommodation is run on
the “one man one seat” —comfortable seat —
system, instead of providing for two men and a
boy on ten inches of hard plank with no back to it.

Five minutes of the Rev. Charles Clarke,
at Melbourne Town Hall, satisfied his friends
that he is the same fascinating dealer in fact,
flavoured with tictr n, who shovelled in the shekels
of the godly and-ungodly alike a dozen years ago.
The failures of many succeeding lecturers have
been bold advertisements for Mr. Clarke, and his
second innings in this country ought to beat the
first—-if he doesn’t spoil things by spouting Im-
perial Federation. Nevertheless it opened rather
tamely on Saturday. R. S. Smythe, who is alleged
to be deeply versed in religious lore, should have
recommended his principal to lie low during Passion
week,
iir ii-' Sit
A mere handful of glowering humanity
lurched into Melbourne Royal on Saturday after-
noon to see Professor Miller take the starch, if he
had any left, out of Duncan Ross, a large, robust
person who set up here as a champion athlete of
the world, and has been failing in the business
about once a week since. The gladiators wrestled
Graeco-Roman style for a weird, shadowy stake
of £2OO. After a furious series of struggles the
Professor wrenched Ross’s arm until it came par-
tially out of gear, whereupon Monsieur Victor
trod the damaged limb back into position as well
as he could, and everybody concerned showed a
disposition to shed each other’s gore. But Miller,
it transpired, had won the match by breaking the
other man’s arm, just as poor drink-destroyed
Whistler used to break his arm, and when this
was explained a great cheer burst from about 40
throats, and Miller went off full of joy at having
won tho hazy sum aforementioned.

a par. anent the New Zealand novelist, B. L. Farjeon,

sperity of his brother—an actor —who is impoverished.
Allow me to inform you for a fact that Farjeon has on
several occasions paid his brother’s passage home, having
forwarded money for the purpose, but the brother has
neglected to avail himself in that and other directions of
the proffered kindness.-— ln The Know,

The Revue des Deux Mondes, just, to hand, lias a
glowing reference to Madame Melba’s Lucia di Lammer-
uioor, from the pen of M. Bellaigue. It is the more valu-
able as being truly critical, not the familiar slaver and
puffery. While praising Madame Melba, the critic, well
known as the best in Paris, walks unmercifully into the
absurdly incongruous costumes of the opera, and the ridi-
culous tropical scenery in Scotland !
Paris critics “ slate” Madame Melba for shaking hands
with the leader of the orchestra when called before the
curtain after “ Lucia di Lammermoor.” They suggest
that she ought rather to have shaken hands with M. ,
Taffanel, the flautist, who accompanied her so magnifi-
cently through the mad scene. The star had better go
round and shake hands with all the people in the boxes.
The Toole luncheon at the Melbourne Oriental, just
fetched up a squad of actors and the familiar identi-
ties who may come under the general designation of
His Gills,” always ready for suction when the chain, is
about. Hopetoun don’t seem to emulate my dear friend,
Tummy.
That fine, mellow actor, John Billington, has accom-
panied Toole to Australia. He was stage-struck through
seeing Maeready in the “ King of the Commons.” Thirty-
years ago John was the Birmingham star. Then he got
to the Adelphi, under Webster, and bore his share in the
“Green Bushes,” “Flowers of the Forest,” and ail those
pieces. His John Peerybingle is Al, and in this he will
support Toole’s Caleb Plummer. His Col. Hardy in “Paul
Pry” will be another treat, and he has good parts in “The
Upper Crust” and nearly all Toole’s pieces. His
wife is an admirable actress, "but does not visit Australia.
Billington is fine and large, a real manly fellow, of the
Henry Edwards stamp.

According to the Hvoao News there is a single lady in
Japan whose dimensions would prove attractive to a well-
known Sydney gentleman who revells at a distance in
quantities of female. She is over Bft. in height, weighs
twenty-four stones, the sole of her foot is seventeen
inches wide, the palm of her hand thirteen, and she is
only sixteen years old ! She is also perfectly well-propor-
tioned and in no way gives the idea of being over-grown.

George Darrell falls foul of Austin Brereton’s statement
that there are and have been no Australian dramatists. A
correspondent sends us a list in which are included the
names of Darrell, Marcus Clarke, Akhurst, Garnet Walcb,
Hopkins, Cooper, R. P. Whitworth, “Demos” Palmer,
“Tommy ” Walker, Fergus Hume, Haddon Chambers, and
Lance Lenton.
J. L. Toole’s father was the civic toast-master in London.
His professional career began, under Chas. Dillon, at the
Queen’s Theatre, Dublin. At Edinburgh he became
acquainted with Irving, when they were stock actors
together in Wyndham’s company. Toole got an engage-
ment at the St. James’s, London, managed by Mrs. Sey-
mour, who afterwards kept house for" Chas. Reade, the
novelist. Dillon then gave Toole a better opening at the
Lyceum, where he opened the same night as Marie Wilton,
afterwards Mrs. Bancroft. The pieces were “ BeJpliegor ”
and “ Perdita Burlesqued.” Toole replaced Wright at the
Adelphi, as Paul Bedford's companion. Then he mounted
to a regular star. He and Irving have not acted together
for very many years, save when, on the eve of Irving
going to America, they acted as Robert Macaire and
Jacques Strop.
We read that Miss Prescott, an American actress,
played lago lately in New York. Rose Evans, Miss Cleve-
land, Louise Pomeroy, Cushman, and a number of other
women, have played Hamlet, but we haven’t heard of a
lady lago before. Mrs. Webb, 90 years ago, used to play
Falstafl in England. Charlotte Cushman’s Romeo was
equal to any man’s. Miss Prescott, as lago, suffered from
her remarkably fine feminine shape, which it was impos-
sible to hide. Otherwise she well delineated the subtlty
of lago.

Bancroft annoys playgoers who love illusion by taking
a call before the curtain just after he has been killed by
Irving at the end of the third act of the “ Dead Heart.”
He ought to remain extended on the stage, and so take
his call with the curtain lifted. He might twiddle his
toe in recognition or wag his ear.

The new drapery in front of the vice-regal box at Mel-
bourne Opera House effectually blocks the view of people
sitting anywhere behind. The space between the gauze
dad pillars was completely filled up with a huge shock of
silver hair on the first night of “ Paul Jones.” People
behind said it belonged to Parkes, and forthwith fell to
discussing his poetry and its effect on Federation.
Did you ever read “ Shakespeare’s England,” by William
Winter, the New York theatrical critic ? It is delicious.
Edmund Yates calls in question the statements made
about Dickens having foretold Irving’s greatness, and
incidentally treats of the humbug in general of raking up
such fancied predictions. In company with Dickens,
Yates saw Irving act Rawdon Scudamore in “Hunted
Down,” and Dickens said, “ That is an excellent imperso-
nation. The man reminds me of your father.” CresWick,
Phelps and Hoskins (the latter Irving’s dramatic tutor)
claim to have discerned the coming Irving, but Creswick
and Phelps were in management while he was starving !
Certainly Billy Hoskins did something for him, for which
Irving remembered his widow. Yates’ father was manager
of the Old Adelphi, London.

scurrilous ; graceful and lively, but without vulgarity.”
That is we. Price. £5OOO a year, with Bank of England
guarantee for ten years’ salary,

“Only for Company.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Joz the Marine told me that he only drank for
company’s sake. A lot of company! Joe swearing
at one end and German Charlie suffering a re-
covery at the other end of the Mount Tophet
hotel’s verandah ! I drink because I like whisky.
Johnny P , the trainer, is like his horses —he
drinks when he is thirsty, and then nothing
stronger than tea. To Mount Tophet with com-
pany, always excepting that of my dog. My
present one is called by the soul-reviving name of
“Presbyterian,” but he answers to “Jock” for
short. And now I can let you know why I keep
the Calvinistic “messan” —only for company. I
love any dog of whatever breed, because when
someone first loved me I had a pup, and such a
pup; let’s see, what was her name? —ah ! “ Cam-
buskenneth,” again a long name, so we called her
“ Cara.”
I.
In the year 1864 I visited the old abbey of
Cambuskenneth, and proceeded to inspect its
beauties. For miles around there is nothing to
compare to them, unless, indeed, they have
changed by evolution. What are the Stirling
wonders and those of St, Ninians and Bannock-
burn to the miracles nursed and storied for some
hundred years ? Never have I, and lam a judge,
seen anything to compare to the breed of wiry
terriers, formerly only to be found in the little
village of Cambuskenneth —and here let me tell
you once for all that in Scotland all words begin-
ning with Cambus, meaning “a bend,” are pro-
nounced as if spelt Cams. At any rate, I went
to Geordie Gray’s cottage and purchased the pet
and christened her “Cam.” Geordie always had
good whisky, and his wife with a solemn face gave
the purchaser a “gless for luck.” Just as I was
returning the quech, the old lady said to me,
“ Yon lass wad dae for yee.” I looked and saw
what I see now, hourly, daily, nightly, whenever
I pat a cur on the head or piek up a pup or hear a
yelp; there in front of my second-sighted eyes
stands Mary Kenneth, only daughter of Kenneth
of Kipponkenneth.
There is a bawbee toll or half-penny ferry
between the Abbey and the shore of Stirling. The
ferryman had advised ms to buy “Cam.,” and was
smiling with delight when I stepped into the boat
and seated myself in the stern beside Miss
Kenneth.
“ Isna’ she pairfect, Maister Phul. ?” “ Truly
said, Joek,” I reply, “ she is simple perfection. I
never saw anything like her.” All this time I
was staring at the lady and she was looking at
“ Cam.”
‘ ‘ Her pedigree’s as long as your’s; her skin’s as
soft as velvet; her een are like stars, and her tem
per, gentle as a doo's ; but, in a fechby, dinna anger
her. For shs’ll snarl and whusk that bit tail o’
hers. '
“ What the -what are you talking about, Jock?”
“The pup, sir. Wnatelse? What did ye think
I was pen tin tae ye?”
“Well, Jock.” said I, “until you mentioned
her tail I was thinking of —”
Then a lady chipped in—“ Please hand me out,
Mr. Philip, for I know your name, although I
have never been introduced to you.” And I
walked up the shore road with Miss Mary and
carried “Cam.”
111.
It was love and my turn to play; I tried a side-
shot. “ You know my name because Jack called
me Phul, and you know my name in full by in-
ference, because you must have heard of even
humble me, probably the only Philip within miles
of the Abbey Craig; and I now know yours
because of the black eyes and Egyptian beauty ;
excuse my plain-speaking, Miss Kenneth, but my
people, the women of them, inherit it also from
the gipsy * Alexanders.’ ”
“ Then we are relatives, or, as Jock would call
it, ‘ far awa freens.” Nevertheless if I met you on
a desert island and had never seen you before I
should know you for a Frandy of Glenfrandy,”
and she laughed.
lam slow of speech like Aaron, and had come
to the cross street before I asked what there could
be in my tout ensemble to stamp me as a man from
near the Oohics.
“ Well,” said she, “I’ll tell you, Mr. Frandy, I
have never yet seen a Frandy wear any clothes
but shepherd’s plaid. The size of the check and
quality of the cloth seem to be the only variations
that climate and visits to Edinburgh entail. They
have always a dog or two after them when
mounted and carry pups as other gentlemen carry
canes. Tiny don’t smoke, and -dear, oh ! dear me,
they DO fancy they are somebody. Perhaps I
should say somebodies.”
“ There’s nothing,” I answered, “ very bad in
all that. The Frandies are, you know ”
Here she laid her head on “ Cam,” and said
quickly: “ Don’t, please don’t, givens the pedi-
gree. Pa says your father has shot it at him for
50 years. I know it by heart. The gravestones
at Tullyochil are crammed with it. Spare me,
Philip, 1 mean Mr. Phil.”
“ Well, it is a consolation to rae that you have
taken to learn it, because you know that like this
pup’s, »s Jock says, it’s as long as yours, and if it
were not for the caution he gave rae as to temper
I should like to say more.”
We had been standing at the corner of the
street. Alister McAlister, of the militia, being
five feet in height and wearing a claymore as long
as himself, had bowed in passing, but Miss Ken-
neth had not noticed him. The Trossaohs ama-
teur coach had galloped by in feeble imitation of
the late Ramsay, of Barnton. It was time for
lunch. I could hear the bugles in the castle.
Daft Bobby Chalmers was interviewing us for a
bawbee, and still I waited for the girl to speak.
At last, very seriously, she almost whispered :
“ Say it.”
There was no Aaron about me this time. Three
words did it effectively.
“I love you.”
You’d hardly believe it, I did not expect it and
was staggered by the rapidity with which she
said, “ I love your dog and —its owner.”
This was the beginning of my only passion
(barring that for “ dugs ”). In my next chapter
I’ll tell you the practical end.
IV.
Six months afterwards Mary Kenneth married
Lord Blairhill, now Earl of Airnsbrae.
V.
Since “ Cam.” grew up and produced litters of
young “ Cams.,” of which “Jock,” alias “Pres-
byterian,” is the lineal descendant, I have visited
Europe, Asia, and the other continents. But
Mary is always there when I see a dog. I have
had, I know not how many, Marys, Maries,
Mollies, Molls, Pollies, Polls, but always one of
the tribe. I lost the strain once when they refused
to ship “Craigie” from Annesley Bay to Suez
during the Abyssinian business, but Geordie Gray
supplied me with “ Abercromby ” (Crummy), and
I started afresh.
VI.
And that is all, except that not so long ago I
met Lady Airnsbrae shopping in a colonial city.
She came straight to me and shook hands, and at
once I asked, “What do you think of this?”
pointing to (it was before “Jock’s” time) “Ochie.”
Her ladyship picked him up, kissed him, and
told me “ I do love your dogs, all, all; and, Mr.

to head “Pretender,” I have often had to use

The Queensland Chief Justice has been
inveighing against the manner in which people
placard fences and buildings with advertisements.
He says that if you want to disgust people with any-
thing make it an eyesore. Look at the Beautiful
Sydney Harbour ! It is now infested by boats on
the sails of which pills are advertised.
The Centennial Magazine must really
look after its names. For instance, Robert Louis
Stevenson’s hero is Mr. Jekyll, and not “ Jeokyll,”
and old Dick Ireland’s second name was Davies
and not Davis—at least we have always read
“ Davies ” in the Law Almanac, which ought to
know; moreover, “Maestrom”is a new word to
us in connection with Poe’s “Tales of Mystery.”
These things we notice at a glance.
The boy-reporter nuisance has broken out
in Tasmania. One daily paper has a youngster of
14 years on its staff.
Longman’s Magazine of a late date con-
tains a story of the loves of a Salvation lass and
a Secularist which does not end in the same
fashion as the Sydney romance.
Said a Hibernian wag of one of the Irish
members found by the judges to be concerned in
none of the charges brought by the Times —“ He
has been found guilty of being innocent.”
Mr. Gladstone says that “lager-beer may
be taken with refreshment and without subse-
quent regret.” Butcher, of Sydney, said that
long ago; Gladstone is a plagiarist as well as a
lager-beer swizzler.
A Western reporter, who, having met with
an accident which shortened his leg, wears an iron
foot, was insulted in a public-house the other day.
What did the Archimedean Lever do ? Why, he
simply put his foot into the fire until it was nearly
red-hot, and then he went for his enemies—hot-
foot.
There is speculation in Melbourne as to
Town Clerk Fitzgibbon’s retirement. Some fancy
City Treasurer Thompson for the post, others
fancy a man like Byron Moore. Fitz. himself
thinks the barristerial qualification indispensable,
but one cannot see a Fitzgibbon among any of the
poor barristers, and it is only a very poor one who
would take his screw.
A Melbourne furniture warehouseman now
advertises his goods by inserting in his window a
placard bearing the notice : “ Wanted, 500 boys
and girls to eat lollies and buns,” and on Saturday
evenings between half-past seven and half-past
eight this gluttonous struggle takes place. Juvenile
creation from all parts assembles at the stated
hour and are arranged audience-like in the window,
an employe supplying them with edibles, whilst
the “ devouration ” is witnessed by crowds of
people outside. Last Saturday evening 5000 buns
and a cwt. of lollies were disposed of in an hour.
Captain Jackson Barry, the N.Z. champion
drawer of the long bow, held at one time partner
shares in a mining claim on the Dunstan. The
claim proved a duffer, and his partners, after
sinking a shaft, abandoned it, although Barry had
still great faith in it. Seeing him standing beside
it one day feeling his bumps, a miner called out,
“ What are you standing over that hole for,
scrat hing your head, Barry?” “What am I
standin’ over this ’ole and scratchin’ my ’ed for? ’
said Barry, “ why, becos there’s millions in it,
that’s why.”
The almost-forgotten name of Sir James
M'Culloch, in Victoria, is recalled by an annonce-
ment of his having recently presided at a London
meeting of the E.S. and A.C. Bank. Probably
he hobnobs with Sir Graham Berry. Best of
friends! Both got the blue ribbon, and by the
same means. Our old friends the People, you
know—with a big P—ha ! ha! Oh, give us a
Duncan Gillies rather than a M‘Culloch or Berry.
What an idol was M'Culloch ! We remember
even a meeting of enthusiastic foreigners in Mel-
bourne in his honor, as the Friend of the People.
Old time revolutionaries spouted in honour of
Makulok.
Dear Bulletin, —I notice that Mr. John
Tasker, of Wellington, N.Z., writes to you,
stating that the old vessel spoken of by me in
yot r issue of the Ist March is the Endeavour
stranded in Dusky Bay. The ship I mentioned
is not the‘same, for the Bruce did not go so far
south as Dusky Bay, and my mother now tells
me that we had to* pick up passengers at the
Haast, and it was there, whilst the Bruce was
being repaired, we saw the old vessel lying
amongst the rocks in the bed of the river, which
was perfectly dry at the time of ottr visit.
■Furthermore, the ship was hardly 400 tons
burden. As regards the Endeavour, my father
when storm-bound at Dusky Bay in the early
sixties, saw the Endeavour, and brought apiece
of her hull to Victoria, which he presented to the
Melbourne Museum, where, I think, it is to be
seen now, I presume there are settlers on the
Haast now, so, perhaps, should this come under
their notice, they may inform us if the remains of
the ;h!ship are still in existence.—Yours, Anon.
Scholarly old David Blair begins, in the
Centennial, sketches of the Victorian Bar, his first
subjects being Aspinall and Evans. He is going
to revive Ireland, Fellows, Michie, Dawson,
Sewell, and all of them. It is a good subject,
with memories of the old Court, the scene of so
many striking tableaux in Melbourne history.
Blair has an immense opinion of Aspinall’s wit,
but it never looked well on paper. He and Michie
wrote a great deal in the Herald, Dr. Evans was
rather more of a journalist. What a sly old fox
was Dick Ireland, with his spectacles pushed up
on his forehead, and his chuckles to the jury. He
seemed to take them all into his confidence,
making a chorus of them; but Fellows towered
head and shoulders above the Bar, and judges, too.
Stawell, Barry, and Williams were helpless in his
hands. Yet he was eminently fair. Now Higin-
botham never had the cast of mind called legal,
the equipment of a first-class barrister or judge,
that is to say, such an altitude in those lines as he
could reach elsewhere. A far greater man than
Fellows, he was absolutely nowhere beside him in
court.
Dear Bulletin, —On a N.S.W. station
owned by a couple of Melbourne men, tanks are
being sunk with station plant, and the wages are
a pound a week for about ten hours a day and
very rough tucker, with an overseer on the bank
to tell you to “fill it up.” The dockers’sd. an
hour is a fool to it. I was working on the place
lately for a few days, lamb-marking. jThe job
concluded, all hands were summoned to the home-
stead to be paid off, when only one old crawler
accepted his “ quid,” the rest refusing most em-
phatically, although I could have done with a
week or two’s work additional, having humped
“ bluey ’’ for three solid months. We all went to
the travellers’ hut “ to wash out our flannens,” in
the vernacular. Close to the water-hole were
several buckets and “billies” in which rabbit-
poison had been mixed, so one of the men went up
and warned the boss that if anyone were poisoned
we’d make things sultry for him, but they were
not removed. The following morning the new-
chum storekeeper came down and told us to
“ get,” and we gat. Nice treatment for a genuine
working man—as you know very well that only
the real searchers for work come off the rivers at
this time of year. Four Chinamen are employed
permanently at this station as gardener, “ house-
maid and laundress,” and rouseabouts, and they
are treated far better than white men. For in-
stance, when lamb-marking we had to walk from
yard to yard, while the boss drove the slant-eyed
Chows over. This is a poor, hungry district, and
the station spoken of is the hungriest of the lot
and as bad as they make ’em.— Jemmy the
Rambler.
At Stuart Hill, the other day, an agricul-
tural gentleman of poor but Irish parents, suc-
cumbed to rum and other complicated influences
aggravated by typhoid in the district mentioned,
and in due time friends and neighbours were in-
vited in to celebrate the occasion, and get respect-
fully but firmly drunk out of esteem for the dead
man and consideration for the surving branches of
the family. The wake was an immense success,
and nothing in the shape of a policeman occurred
to mar the ardent conviviality or interrupt the
fighting when things began to step high and get in
swing with the con impetnoso flow of soul and
the elegant heap of jigs imparted to the pro-
ceedings by a Fenian cripple armed with a three-
stringed fiddle and a power of crude enthusiasm.
Had somebody of uncommon size and strength,
and power of will, been appointed to keep
sober and overlook the festivities in the in-
terest of deceased, there would have been
no necessity for the description we are writing,
more in sorrow than in anger, but no such office
of trust was created, and the wake was per-
mitted to extend beyond the requirements of
the hour and the necessities of the occasion, in
consequence of which nobody could sober up in
time for the funeral, and the solemn obsequies
we r e conducted in an atmosphere of departed
spirits reminiscent of a superannuated brewery.
The mourners got old Morrissey’s coffin into the
hearse at the appointed hour, however, and the
{nocession started, the sorrowing friends and re-
ations loading themselves into tip-carts and wood-
drays and careering intemperately through the
dust after the late lamented Michael-—who got
away first from scratch and was cutting out the
running, the hearse being half-a-mile ahead most
of the time, whilst the tail of the funeral was
getting interlocked and overturned on the road-
side. Throughout all these tribulations the
blighted mourners maintained a becoming air of
reverential solemnity that could not be anything
but gratifying to deceased, and when the coffin
was lowered into the grave and “ his riverence ”
had said his say, an aged but vigorous female,
under commission, lifted up her harrowing,
scratched-cat, contralto yell, and lamented the
occasion in admirable style, considering that she
was working a dead-horse, the job having already
been paid for in whisky, and the friends and
relations stood about and shed tears over the scene
and commiserated with each other in a way that
was most touching and pathetic. The mourners
were somewhat subdued and indulgent in inward
grief when they returned to the home of the late
lamented, and several had unloaded themselves
and advanced into the house when the rest of the
funeral was startled to hear a discordant series of
shrieks, followed by calls for police assistance and
the benefit of clergy, and simultaneously three
men broke out of the house via windows and the
door and fled into the wood in pursuit of the pro-
tection of various saints and other comforts of
religion, and on the relations going into the
kitchen they discovered the deceased gentleman,
Michael Morrissey, dressed in his shroud and
propped against the mantle-shelf, with his back to
the fire and his old black pipe in his mouth. Then
arose the righteous tempestuousness and expres-
sions of rage already mentioned, and the sons and
heirs of deceased went out with loaded sticks,
demanding vengeance and seeking someone to
kill, but finding him not. Michael Morrissey’s
funeral had to happen all over again. Two de-
praved jokers, under the influence of Milesian
whisky and an inspiration of the Devil, had
removed deceased from his coffin after he had
been screwed down, and whilst the sounds of
revelry were yet heal'd in the farm-house had sub-
stituted bricks and empty bottles and hidden thelale
lamented under a bed, so that all the expense, and
the wailing at the grave, and the prayers of his
reverence had been expended over a heap of dead
marines. Let us hero leave our emotions to the
imagination of the reader.

pamphlet dealing with the Father Damien asper-
sions.

in-law, was for many years clerk of eourts at
Ballarat.
Nelson P. Whitelocke, of the Barrier
Miner, charges the Shook of Gamp-bridge with
bigamy.! This is a startler.
Douglas B. W. &c., &c., Sladen was, at
latest advices, in Japan, visiting another distin-
guished poet, Edwin Arnold,
Lord Carington, it is cabled, is to be made
an Earl. It is well that we Australians should
know exactly what they make earls of.
The Pope has consented to the Emperor
of Austria’s nephew and heir-presurnptive marry-
ing the widow of his (the Emperor’s) late son.
E. H. Taylor (liabilities, £200,000 or so)
was, a few years ago, in the employ of a Sydney
firm of universal providers at two pounds a week.
Matthew Burnett, the “Yorkshire Evange-
list,” took tea the week before last at Chief
Justice Way’s, with Sir Henry Parkes and Lord
Kintore.
Pasteur is partially paralysed. It may
prevent misconception if we state that he was
attacked before he heard of Lucy Wilkinson’s in-
tention to visit him.
The all-round interest shown just now by
the cok*ial press in the fortunes of the Bennett
family must be highly interesting to the various
members thereof.
A London writer asserts that not Edmund
Yates but T. G. Bowles, who founded Vanity
Fair as a shilling journal in ’6B, was the father of
“ society ” journalism.
One of the latest financial failures of Lon-
don owned “ Carruthers-Gould ” as a name. Now,
could anybody be a success so ticketed ? It sug-
gests a plated article.
Princess Dolgorouki, the rightful wife of
the late Czar, lives in France and dare not visit
Russia. Her two sons, by the late Czar, are
French citizens, liable to the conscription.
Parkes is reported to have told Gillies that
his forthcoming “autobiography” will be his
monument. In that case, how about “ Hie Jactt
Parkes ” as a suitable inscription ?”

“ Shilly-shally.”
Gaunson has been misleading a Melbourne
Bench by attributing “Tinker’s Damn” to the
Iron Duke of Wellington, but here is the expres-
sion he refers to —“ I don’t care a twopenny damn
for the caricatures.”
Lord Hampden (ex-Bpeaker Brand of the
House of Commons) now retails his own butter
and cheese. He has two shops in prominent parts
of London and half-a-dozen carts with his name
on call for orders.
Chanteloup, of Montreal, a wealthy French-
Canadian brass-founder, has left the whole of his
large fortune, estimated at £200,000 to his em-
ployes. They will continue the business as a
joint-stock concern.
Editor Fitchett, of Melbourne D.T.,
started life as a carrying and forwarding agent’s
clerk, at Geelong. He was a dab at waybills.
This accounts for the weighty—not to say heavy-
style of his current leaders.
Lord Lamington, member for North St.
Pancras, who died lately, and who was touring
the colonies when the defeat of the Gladstone
Ministry compelled him suddenly to return, was
the Buckhurst of “Coningsby.”

a number of kdted Higlandmen danced a reel.

man.

poet and paperhanger, who upset Primate Barry’s
lecture to the working men at Lambeth Baths.

Barry in the rumpus.

The Baroness Burdett-Coutts and her
Bartlett lately celebrated the ninth anniversary
of their wedding. One of the guests, by way of
congratulating the host, reminded him that the
Iron Duke was once a suitor for the hand of the
Baroness. Whereupon another guest, intending
to be complimentary, playfully said—“ But Bart-
lett was not born then.”
Rev. Charles Clarke, just back from
England, is the same admirable clerical comedian.
He would be excellent as some burlesque female
character. He ought never to have been a parson,
but should have introduced himself to us as
Affable Hawk, or any of Charles Matthews’
characters. We might see him in them yet—or
get him to publicly sing “The Hen Convention.” '
Sir Charles Gavan Duffy means to get out
his autobiography, which will be interesting—
especially to Victorians. He is thrice a widower.
Who’d have thought that such a squeaking atomy
bad so much blood in him? “ Mr. Squeaker!”
Why, it Was just like an old woman. But the
stuff in his speeches was good. He would write
out what he had to say, and give absolutely every
word from memory, as did Bishop Moorhouse.
Casey thought he could do it. Ho gave a Sand-
hurst speech beforehand to the Age, which, how-
ever took the precaution of sending up two
reporters. About nine o’clock came down a tele-
gram : “ He hasn't spoken one word of it!”

The papers are talking of the N.S.W.
Opposition electing a new leader. A party never
elects a leader—he elects himself.

are all Socialists—three Socialist members. No
wonder the Despot wakes and quakes.

they say, have been imported to Liverpool and
other English ports. There they stick. Patriotic
masters !

The numerical progress of Socialism in
Germany is astounding. In 1867, at Berlin, 07
socialists voted ;in 1887, 774,128; in 1890, the full
poll is not yet known to us, but will probably
reach 1,500,000,
A London paper proposes that a statue be
erected out of the funds of the Royal Society to
“ the memory of the late lamented Richard Pigott,
in consideration of his important services in the
establishment of truth.”
The Charters Towers branch of the Natives’
Association has resolved that Sir John Bray’s
statement at the Federation Conference, that
Australian natives are not Republicans, does not
receive their endorsement.

Reverend Unitarian Walters, is appar-
ently “on the fence” as to George’s doctrines.
Now, we call upon him as one of the few students
of modern literature in Sydney’s pulpits to get
down on the Land-nationalisation-ram-Compensa-
tion side of the panel.
Carlyle wrote about the German Emperor
and his Working Men’s Congress in the “French
Revolution.” Turn up that passage where ho
refers to the farmer asking his pigs with what
sauce he shall cook them. Says the pig : “We
don’t want to be eaten.”
The Christchurch (N.Z.) Telegraph re-
cently contained an article in which it was stated
that W. W. Lyght, visiting that city as the
alleged representative of the American Knights of
Labour, had shown no proof that ho was the
accredited agent of that body.
Henry George has been twitting David
Syme, of the Age, with going back on his Land-
nationalisation doctrines of a dozen years ago. It
is as though the American philosoper were to start
finding faults in the orginal Ananias, so Mel-
bourne passes the incident by with a casual snort.
In the Times’ report of W. H. Smith’s
resolution as to the Parnell letters, the word
“forged,” used by Old Morality, was carefully
left out. The journal has never used that word
in connection with the Pigott effusions. The
Australian daily press lias thus high, offensively
high, example for its frequent little suppressions
of awkward facts.
The Bulletin would respectfully suggest
to the Single-tax League that they should elect
Mrs. Garland president, in place of her hubby.
Both have read “Progress and Poverty,” but she
understands it. At all events, she knows enough
about its doctrine not to give the “cause” away
the first time she is asked a leading question.
Which can’t be said for Garland, M.P.
Salomons says it is the landless who are
Single-taxers. Now, as a statement of a general
law, the remark might be as accurate as we can
expect that of a lawyer to be, but in particular in-
stances it is wrong. Take Jack Haynes, for example.
He’s a Single-taxer; at least, he says he is. And
we know he has an estate. But, jesting apart, the
landless are in the vast majority, and Salomons’
statement is an argument against, instead of, for
private property in land.
Mr. Caine, the Unionist Whip, has written:
“I cannot find language to express my horror of
the foul conspiracy to which Mr. Parnell has been
subjected, or ray opinion of the conduct of the
Times in lending itself to these infamous libels on
the flimsy evidence furnished to them of their
supposed genuineness. Mr. Parnell must be held
to have come through the inquiry practically un-
scathed.” Coming from the enemy, this letter
ought to be jam for the Uncrowned King.
It is no secret that Henry George has been
grievously disappointed to find the N.S. Wales
Single-tax party what it is—numerically, mentally
and financially. He had conceived a very different
idea of its strength, partly from Garland’s, M.P.,
skiting when on his recent “tour” in the States,
and partly from an official letter of Secretary
Meggy’s, published in the New York Standard,
in which it was state ; d that “the Single-taxers
bear the balance of political power in the colony !”
On that historical occasion when Purves
stood up and wept before the A.N.A. delegates he
stated that it “had been the dream of his life to
become a leader of men ” and that his dream had
been realised in his connection with the little
mutual admiration crowd of Natives. Purves is
easily satisfied. A cynical stranger, attending an
A.N.A. Conference and studying the quality of
the jabber thrown off thereat, would conclude that
the inflated Q.C. was not so much a leader of men,
as boss over a lot of boys.
Henry George, at Melbourne, in one of his
anti-prqtectionist flings, wanted to know how
Protection would benefit the tram-conductors.
We’ll tell him. But for the existence of factories
in Melbourne the unemployed th e re would increase
to a twenty-fold their present numbers, and the
applicants for train vacancies would assuredly
multiply in the same ratio. With ten or twenty
men to pick from where there are now one or two,
would present rate of wages in the train service be
maintained? Not much, Mr. George. Just ask
Sprigg.
“ If the Christian religion is sound, then
Freetrade must eventually prevail. I believe in
the justice, freedom, and truth established by the
common Christian religion, and I feel that it is
impossible for Protection to prevail.” Thus doth
Pope Parkes excommunicate the wicked and un-
regenerate of a world run on the principle of
“Fat or be Eaten.” Thus doth he cast into
outer darkness where there is weeping and gnash-
ing of teeth those who having been hit on the
point of the jaw refuse to turn the other cheek to
Calico-Jemmy, the smiter.
Duncan Gillies has his lucid intervals and
redeeming points. A couple of Victorian senators,
one of whom keeps a little pub., waited upon
Duncan to ask him what he could do in the
matter of carrying Chows, at a reduced rate, to
the Sandhurst Fair. The fat little Premier re-
plied that he would do nothing at all, after which
the whisky man and his pals retired in great con-
fusion. 'I his is the first time that any Australian
Government has been asked by alleged white men
to grant special concessions to the slant-eyed
leper—except in the way of enabling him to leave
the country —and we trust it won’t occur again.
The report that a St. Petersburg “social-
ist ” has committed suicide, after confessing that
he was chosen by lot to blow up the Czar, is a lie
on the face of it. In the first place, no socialist
enters into plots for assassination —not even of the
Czar. In the next place, no man would kill him-
self rather than kill the Czar, not under any cir-
cumstances. Moreover, it is beginning to be
understood that most of these Nihilist—not
Socialist —plots are invented by the Russian police
to the order of his Most Sacred Majesty. It is
necessary to find constant excuses for his chronic
condition of funk. The very latest cablegram
about Mr. Immensikoff Romanoff is that it is
untrue he has been poisoned as alleged—he is
“only suffering from fainting-fits.”
Colonel Stoffel's recently-published pair
phlet has raised once more the question whet he-
Germany did wisely in depriving France of Alsace
and Lorraine, and whether, had the conquerors
been content to receive a larger indemnity in
money, while leaving French territory intact,
peace and goodwill between the two nations might
not have been possible in lieu of deadly animosity,
which may at any moment take the form of war.
Prince Bismarck’s organ, the North German
Gazette, replies that in the course of three cen-
turies France attacked Germany no less than 20
times, and that it would be ridiculous to trust to
French forbearance when it was so much more
simple to take up a commanding military position
and to extend the German frontier as far as the
Vosges. It is quite a mistake to suppose that the
military party in Germany was alone in favour of
feizing Alsace and Lorraine—that is to say, the
fortresses of Strasburg and Metz. Prince Bis-
marck thought, equally with Count Moltke, that
the French, who had not forgiven the Prussians
for the victory of Sadowa, which in no way con-
cerned the interests of France, would never pardon
the defeat of Sedan, and that whatever the con-
ditions of peace might be, they would, in five, ten,
twenty years, as soon as they found themselves
strong enough, recommence the war.

# # *
What trouble the clergy have in keeping
the toes of all the Australian judges up to the
line ! Here’s Boucaut, in ■' delaide, kicking over
the straps. Chief-Justice Hanson there wrote a
hook of Strauss, mixed with Renan. As for Vic-
toria—why, Orthodoxy has scarce a judge. Chief
Justice Higinhotham is—well, all that is shocking.
Then there is Judge Williams—worse, if possible.
Hodges, to be sure, has a little of the Stawell tar-
brush ; Webb is a Wesleyan, but visible in the
dress-circle when a good play is on; Holroyd is
never discerned in any Ebenezer work; A’Beckett
is a negation; Hood never takes the chair at a
missionary meeting. Oh, for another Judge
Fellows, who built the Queenscliff Church ! But,
after all, what sort of an oyster-and-porter church-
man was he ? Then there was Wilberforce
Stephen, Chancellor of the Church of England—he
sold it dead as prime promoter of the godless
Education Act. And Sir Redmond Barry—dear,
old, gay Sir Redmond ! Why, he was a Sir George
Bowen.
N.S. Wales is somewhat better off. Darley,
C.J., is starchy and orthodox; Foster, J., is abso-
lutely pious; Stephen, Innes and Manning are all
pretty good churchmen ; Windeyer alone has a
strongly liberal kink, and what a perfect daisy he
is, taken all round ! Chief Justice Martin had no
religious prejudices of any kind. Nor is there
much piety in Chief Justice Lilloy. No really
pious man could write such wicked letters to
“ dear Stirling.”

The first step towards shifting the centre
of the Sydney wool-trade has been taken. Wool
stores are to be erected right alongside Admiralty
House, North Shore—nice, this, for the Admiral.
The persons concerned in the enterprise acquired
the site for £15,000 ; some time ago it was offered
the Government at £lO,OOO.

to meet Mr. Blaiklock; the local-optionist, in
debate. But then how could Blaiklock possibly
discuss whisky?

A certain haughty swell was recently seen
pic nicking down Sydney harbour with a live lord
at the very hour when the remains of his sister

# # *
The fire-bell was put down in Melbourne
as a nuisance, but now they have the harsh c'angour
of St. Raul’s Cathedral bells in the midst of the
city. This hideous peal is warranted to kill any
sick person within a half-mile radius. Why should
the tire-bell be denied a license accorded to the
hell-fire-bell ?
An idea strikes us : Let the clergy lay on
the telephone to their customers’ houses and shout
“ Hello ! ” when it’s time to go to church.
« * *
Some time back a Sydney citizen, one
Campbell, committed an act that should, in the
good old times, have brought him much glory,
but which through the degeneracy of modern
ideas has procured for his memory nothing
but an infinity of curses. He left a peal
of bells to St. Phillip’s Church with an un-
derstanding that they were to be rung so
many times every week. The good clergyman
who runs the show, not having the sense to let
such a barbarous injunction fall into abeyance, has
solemnly carried it out. On Good Friday they
were jangled at all hours, Sunday the same, and
even on Monday the residents around Church
Hill were tormented by these awful bells
making the morning air hideous. Surely
this parsonic fiend should be brought under
some sort of law. Let him set the following verses
to the horror of his bells :
Mr. Campbell! Mr. Campbell ! when you twang
your harp and sing,
Do you think what mortals utter when those bells
of yours they ring ?
When the parson (Devil take him !) has them
jangled, pealed, and slammed.
Mr. Campbell! Mr. Campbell ! people say may
you be d d.
Mr. Campbell! Mr. Campbell ! where you’re
snuffling saintly airs
Do they call with bell discordant for the just to
to come to prayers ?
Is the holy calm of Heaven thus bedeafened out
of tune
Till the flashing jasper pavement with bad words
is all bestrewn ?
Mr. Campbell! Mr. Campbell! in your own time
you were wise,
For you had them hung and swung there after
you’d left for the skies.
Win our gratitude then for us, even though its
rather late;
Get the Lord to send a message that he wants
that parson straight.
# # #
A Sydney cheap draper says that although
he has hundreds of pounds’ worth of goods stolen
from his shop in Oxford street he never prosecutes.
Women are the chief offenders, and their favourite
trick is to put their babies down and lift some
article of drapery when taking up the little
beauty. The Bulletin has at last ascertained
what babies are good for. Innocence is a first-
class thing to steal with.
* # #
Two well-to-do Melbourne women have
been arrested for kleptomania—in company—at
least, we suppose it will be called kleptomania, but
the barrister will be puzzled. “Look here, you
know, two kleptomaniacs together ; it stems hope-
less.” One of the women is a widow, owning a
terrace of houses ; the other is the wife of a pros-
perous butcher. They are alleged to have robbed
drapers’ shops.

would be £150,000, and the annual interest on

Conflicting stories as ti the condition of
the widow of the late Lord “Gumboil” Cairns are
published in the London papers. Some say that
the aforesaid condition is “interesting,” while
others deny that there is any possibility of an
heir-apparent, therefore Mr. Herbert Cairns may
assume the title whenever he choose. If Herbert
really has cause to bo afraid of an interloping
little stranger, we recommend him to hedge a bit.
The natural odds are only slightly in favour of a
boy, so if lie likes to offer £15,000 to £lO,OOO
against his chance of succession, no doubt some of
the old nobility will book it. One of our Austra-
lian governors, for instance, is fond of a sporting
wager. £lO,OOO would partially console Herbert
for the birth of his nephew, and if the babe fell an
early victim to convulsions, “ nunky ” would come
in for the title before he had “ blewed” the wager.
What a splendid double to bring off !

Seven-and-a-half-millions sterling has been
offered by an English syndicate for the Mount
Morgan mine. Clutch, boys, clutch, even as an
electric hair-trigger rat-trap run by essence of
greased chain-lightning cluteheth. The noble
British public will be sorry before you are.
# # #
A great saint has just departed from Aus-
tralia “ for his health.” Which reminds us of an
interesting story about a lighthouse keeper’s
daughter. The yarn is not published in the
orthodox versions of the gospel, and perhaps there
was nothing in it. On the other hand, perhaps
there was. You can’t expect people to be “good ”
all round.
* # #
A European lady, from China, has brought
over to Melbourne from China her Celestial nurse,
who carries the baby after her along the Collins-
street Block. The nurse is a Chinese woman
about 40 years old, in full Celestial costume, with
trousers, but no hat, having her hair done up in
picturesque fashion with silver hairpins in an
Italian-looking style. Which reminds us that a
fine-looking South Sea Island woman is staying at
a Sydney coffee-palace, where she walks about in a
print frock, out A to Grecque, on bare feet, with an
ease and dignified innocence that Alls the corseted
waitresses with envy and melts the breasts of the
susceptible pressmen who dine there and pick
their teeth outside the Athenaeum.
A very smart thing has been done up
South. A lot of the bhoys determined to start
a paper and sent round a canvasser to get
shares subscribed. This canvasser induced all the
subscribers to entrust him with their proxies for
the election of directors. The consequence is
that he appointed himself to something snug and
constructed a board of directors whose names
read like a fist of police-arrests at Ballyhooley fair.
.i•vrc w n v ,
A certain Kb. W. Cabinet Munster is the
source of much amusement to the officials in his
Department. His decisions, if nothing else, are
novel and original. Nothing is too irrelevant to
receive his “ approval ” or “concurrence.” One
recommendation for giving a man a billet is that
it has been “promised.” The natural death of
one official and the suicide of another both get his
“approval.” If a list of applicants for employ-
ment is sent to him he marks it “ approved.” He
concurs in even an adverse newspaper criticism of
himself. He gloats over the newspaper reports of
his own speeches, and not only marks them “ ap-
proved,” but endorses them as “an intelligent
account of the case.” The most trivial incident
is recorded in the daily press, so desirous is he of
appearing in print. He has a high notion of what
is “proper and becoming to a Minister of the
Crown.” So, in imitation of Gov. Carington, it
is supposed, lie makes the messengers fly before
him to clear the way at his entrances and his exits.
If he has to ascend the G.P.O. tower with Lord
Mullett or the Duke of Cockatoo, the tower is
closed to the public, and all the messengers, &0.,
in Sunday attire have to
be in readiness to receive
them.

A new poet named Macartney has burst
on the astonished universe. He is an Imperialist,
a war-gusher, and just in time to catch the laurels
falling from the brows of Tennyson. Hear him in
a lay of the Soudan
The chaste Diana wears the Robe
Of modesty again,
And cannot gaze upon the sleep
Of fourteen thousand men.
While all the startled stars on high,
Have hid themselves in fright,
Except a few, who stare unshamed
Like strumpets of the night.

* # #
If you want to shock the Rev. Garnsey
or some other high-toned and incensed and gilded
Sydney Ritualist, ask him if he’s aware that a
former president of the English Union is in Ber-
rima prison. And, then, when he has writhed
sufficiently, soothe him gently with the informa-
tion that it is only as clerk and schoolmaster, and
not as a graduated sinner, that the sainted
functionary “does ” Berrima.
* * # .
The late Dr. Littledale, the “scholar” of
the Ritualists, was the best composer in all Eng-
land of those fearful and wondrous things called
acrostics. He was L'AIM of the World. There
isn’t much, difference, after all, between puzzles
and problems in ecclesiastical scholarship. While
exalted prelates like Moran and Camidge have
nothing better to do than to propound conun-
drums to one another we’ll wager that no one can
answer this riddle: What is the present use of the
Church ?
• # *
A Melbourne religious newspaper whines
over the poor clergy who only get £3OO a year,
and asks for donations of books for Jhem, be-
cause they cannot afford them. We once heard a
clergyman, in the Presbyterian Assembly, call
£5OO a year “miserable !” It was the Rev. Dods,
Dr. Strong’s coadjutor, now gone home to some-
thing more substantial. Rev. Quick, Wesleyan
minister, wrote in the Melbourne Daily Tele-
graph some time ago that a clergyman could not
live properly on £3OO a year. The meek and lowly
one couldn’t keep his servants, drawing-room, and
the indispensable buggy, we suppose.
* * *
The Anglican Bishop of Melbourne has
been meekly suggesting to local teetotallers that,
although abstainers are the soul of the good cause,
“ moderate drinkers ” are also a valuable moral
check on the spread of jim-jams. Brother Eield
]’ lowers Goe is a moderate drinker, you will ob-
serve. He lowers his two glasses of beer at dinner
with a pious and unworldly gurgle that is soothing
to the ear in wai’m weather, and he can supple-
ment the tanglefoot by just one glass-—“ a sma-all
glass-s, if you please ” —of wine, on special oc-
casions. The Bishop doesn’t smoke, however,
because he has never felt the want of it, do you
know. A parson with Gee’s private means, and
neither chick nor child to worry him, has no parti-
cular call to seek nepenthe in a pipe of ruby twist,
perhaps. But a clergyman friend of Goe’s, who
had great trouble in getting to sleep at nights,
was advised to try one cigar before retiring to his
bedroom, and since then the reverend gentleman
sleeps, Oh !so soundly. A doosid pretty story,
don’t you think ?
* * *
A sandy-haired man with freckles poured
Ins woes into the sympathising ear of a portly and
well-groomed parson, on board a Sydney tram
from a fashionable suburb. The freckled man
carried in his hand an advertisement for a servant,
and on the misguided notions of duty held by the
last occupant of the post he descanted with vigour:
—“Yes, sir, she was only in the house three days.
During that time she made a beefsteak-pie a dog
would bark at, burnt the fronts out of two of my
shirts, trimmed her boot-heels with my best hollow-
ground razor, and stayed out all night at a dancing-
house to which she went in a complete suit of my
wife s clothes. By the way, I expect you know
her, as lam told she sings in your choir?” “Oh !
then I suppose it is Miss ——you are speaking of.
I had a note from her this morning, in which she
asks me for consolation, having been compelled to
leave a situation in which she was not appreciated.
I am now on my way to perform that pastoral
duty. Good morning, my dear sir. Good morn-
ing.”
* # *
A newly-elected provincial Mayor now
on a visit to Sydney, was some years ago cook on
board a Murray River steamer. It so happened
that a man of the same surname was cook on
another steamer and was drowned. Rumour,
however, stated that the former was the drowned
man and the supposed widow went into mourn-
ing ; but consider the alteration of her feelings
when the mistake was discovered ! The meeting
between the loving couple upon the; local wharf
on the return of the steamer by which the now
Mayor travelled will never be forgotten by the
onlookers.

A London paper states that the Countess
of Hopetoun left Melbourne and took refuge in
Tasmania because she found that so many of her
courtiers were of the light-fingered tribe ! Time’s
whirligig brings its revenges. People go to Van
Diemen’s Land to find honest men and women !
* * *
Is there anybody hereabouts who knows
Tongan ? An alleged Tongan scholar came along
to this office the other day with a missionary-book
printed in that language and proceeded to show us
what the sacred volume was stuffed with. It
encouraged the niggers, he said, in all sorts of
hideous immorality—and if his interpretation of
its contents is to be accepted, the good mis-
sionaries’ little volume must certainly bo regarded
as a startler.

According to the Paris correspondent of
the Sydney Morning Herald the Dominican
order of R.C. priests “still, as in the past, believes
in the righteousness of the faggot and its efficacy
in the case of opponents of the Papacy, and the
doctrines promulgated by the Papal Church.”
We must keep ’em out of Australia or they’ll be
burning John Haynes.
* * *
Dean Swift placed Lilliput about where
Tasmania in after years was discovered. The
Dean was, after all, not so far out in his latitude.
Lilliput, on current maps, exists a few miles away
from the main Sydney and Melbourne trunk rail-
way, and there the veritable Gulliver is to be seen
daily. But whether the place owes its name to
the man or the man takes his from the place is
doubtful. Anyhow, the fact remains that Lilliput
is in Victoria, and that Gulliver lives there.

* * *
Lady Palmer, widow of Sir Jaraeo F.
Palmer, first Legislative Council President of
Victoria, has just died in England, aged 83. The
old fellow was a doctor, but had to manufacture
ginger-beer for a living, because the high tide of
influenza, typhoid, and diphtheria hadn’t then
arrived. They say he even hawked ginger-beer
through the streets, pushing a truck. He had a
miserly reputation, but he was President of the
Melbourne Hospital, and the conclusion arrived
at was that he did good by stealth. He lived on
the Hawthorne Hill, bought and cut up by old
Coppin, as St. James’ Park. Of course the St.
James was Palmer.
# * *
Dr. Youl, who sits on Melbourne corpses
and on live people when they require it, was
recently lecturing the police for allowing a prisoner
to secrete a revolver in his cell. “Suppose he had
come into Court, you know, and shot one of the
magistrates 1” said Youl. The extreme awfulness
of this possibility struck the whole Coort dumb
for several seconds, and the policemen present
reeled and staggered on their large, flat feet. At
length the silence was broken by a_ still, small
voice from the back benches, exclaiming, with
subdued fervour—“And a good job, too.”

. Story from abroad for the benefit of Dr.
Rorke, of Sydney. At Dublin city sessions a
juror prayed to be excused on account of ill-
health and handed up an envelope containing
what the juror evidently thought to be a medical
certificate of illness. It was read out and ran
thus : “ This man lias been asking for a certificate
that he is unable to serve as a juror. I don’t
know whether he is a knave or fool, but he has
very little brains and reeks of porter.”
The Victorian Parliamentary refreshment-
room keeper, in Melbourne, must have a bonus of
£6OO a year, or they cannot get a boniface for it.
Then there is the tennis - court —bonus again.
Billiard-room —another bonus. Nice club for poor
men with only a precarious £3OO a year. It costs
the price of two members to keep the refreshment-
room going. But the loss isn’t on the grog—no
fear. What the lessee complains of is the half-
penny worth of bread to all the sack. Tuesday’s
meats do coldly furnish forth Thursday’s tables,
and the members won’t have it. Even if one of
them spoils a leg of mutton to his own cheek, it
must be fresh.

please hand me that bit of epiglottis.”

“ Demos’ Maiden Aunt” is the title of a
trenchant article, by Grant Allen, in the Uni-
versal Review, a powerful sarcasm on the solici-
tude of the Middle Class, the bourgeois, about the
morals of the Lower Orders. We have a nauseous
quantity of this kind of thing in Australia, and
Allen says the people will have their amusements
in spite of that middle class, to which the “ vice
of Puritanism ”is confined. His typical Aunt
Martha is closely related to the Nonconformist
grocer everybody knows him. Sandy ! Mr.
Sanders, the fellow with the Sugar
Have you suitably doctored the treacle and coasipore?
Then down on your marrowbones, here on the floor.
Oh, the wickedness of the Lower Orders !
