# * *
A correspondent writes ;
I have not seen the London World’s “Celebrities at
Home” article with B. L. Farjeon as its subject, and 1
don’t therefore know whether any reference is made to
the circumstance that the novelist is himself the hero of
as pretty a bit of romance as everhe created. Years ago,
when a hard-working pressman in N.Z., he was shown a
portrait of Joe (“ Rip Van Winkle ”) Jefferson’s daughter.
Then and there, fascinated by the charm of the face, he
tell in love with, and vowed to marry the original, if she
were “ free and available.” Nothing appeared more un-
likely at the time than that he would ever realise his vow.
But he did.
# * *
Some Canadian Mormons have raised the
legal point that there is not one English law
against polygamy ; but that the Empire sanctions
it, as* in the case of her Mahommedan subjects.
The Courts have reserved j udgment on the point,
thus admitting it has a prima-facie importance.
The result will, no doubt, be looked for with some
degree of anxiety by prominent Sydney society
men who at present are compelled, in deference
to the social proprieties, to distribute their harems
under several roof-trees. It would be so much
cheaper to run a consolidated household that they
need not mind the trifling preliminary expense of
matrimonially hitching on to Delilah and Camille
and putting ’em on the same connubial ration-
sheet as the original Mary Ann.

milk also.

English women are now purchasing men's
saddles for their own use. A London saddler says

Now that Melbourne has caught the
influenza-scare nervous people are afraid to use
the cable tramways, for fear of the grip-man.
The Pentridge gaol warders strike against
a regulation that any man asleep on duty is to b e
dismissed. As the first watchman says to Dog-
bery “We’ll rather sleep than talk, we know
what belongs to a watch.”
The proposed Palestine railway will run
.'through Hebron, Jerusalem, Jericho, Nazareth
and Galilee to Damascus. The sentimental are
objecting to it; nothing so iconoclastic has been
known since Mark Twain’s “Innocents Abroad.”
Biggar, on first entering the Commons,
was complimented by a Radical friend on the
character of his votes. “How,” said his friend,
“ do you manage so frequently to be in the right ?”
“ Well,” replied Mr. Biggar, with a merry twinkle
in his eye, “I always follow to the lobby that
bald-head (pointing to Sir Wilfrid Lawson’s head-
piece), and then I know I am always right.”
When Single-tax Foxall was debating with
Tom Rose in the Protestant Hall, Sydney, he
quoted Carlyle* saying that “all mankind were
divided into three classes —workers, beggars, and
robbers.” Hereupon some one in the audienct
called out: “And what class do you belong to?”
“ The robbers,” replied Foxall, “and I want you
to deprive me of the opportunity of robbing. ”
THE FEDERATION OAK.
[n the Gardens of the Parliament at Melbourne
can be seen,
By anyone who seeks it out, a sapling fresh and
green;
And may it ne’er be stricken by a vandal axe-
man’s stroke,
But live to be a landmark great—a Federation
Oak.
’Twas planted by Sir ’Enry on a pleasant autumn
day,
A little crowd of windbags gathered round and
blew “ Hooray 1”
When ’Enry with a pickaxe gave the ground a
feeble poke,
Where a place was made already for the Federa-
tion Oak.
He used to think Australia was simply New
South Wales;
To express his scorn for Melbourne our vocabulary
fails.
But now he goes there often (on the spree, the
giddy bloke !)
And, to show he loves his neighbours, plants a
Federation Oak.
If his patriotic ardour gets a little time to cool,
He slates about the Murray and begins to play
the fool;
So no man pays attention to the gushing words he
spoke
At the Conference whose fruits will be the acorns
of the oak.
Ah! tend that sapling carefully, x>rotect it from the
boy
E’er ready with his penknife Nature’s wonders to
destroy ;
Though the talking-match in Melbourne was but
’Enry’s little joke,
In Austral's heart is striking deep the Federation
Oak.
It’s trunk shall be colossal and its branches high
shall wave
When the present generation lies forgotten in the
grave ;
The peaceful rain of wisdom round the sapling’s
roots shall soak,
Till we drink, “Our Free Republic!” ’neath the
Federation Oak.
The tree Sir ’Enry planted in its boyhood fresh
and green—
When the “leading men’s” religion was the
worship of the Queen,
And the drowsy country listened to their lying,
“ loyal ” croak—
May it grow to be respected as the Federation
Oak !

and her best cold-weather complexion.
“GRIP.”
Now tins influenza scare
Is a stroke of fortune rare
For a lot of men who ne’er,
Get the Grip;
All the “ symptoms ” they display.
And in bed are thought to stay,
But they tripit down the bay
On a ship.
That’s a tip
For the clerks who stop away
With the Grip.
Its a blessed thing to sneeze
Every boy at school agrees,
Then at once he murmurs “ Please,
I’ye the Grip;
I can feel it in the head,
Both my legs are lumps of lead.
And my eyes are getting red—
May I skip ?”
That’s the tip,
For on dainties they are fed
With the Grip.
The husband of a wife
Who’s the worry of his life
Gets a little rest from strife
Through the Grip ;
He talks about the scare
Till she wants a change of air,
Then he goes upon the “ tear ”
Gay old rip!
That’s a tip.
Husbands, offer up a prayer
For the Grip.
It behoves us to acquaint
Parties down with this complaint
That the whisky-bottle ain’t
G od for Grip ;
If you do not wish to die,
On your backs you ought to lie
Till you get about as “ dry ”
As a chip,
That’s the tip—
Oh ! it makes the boozers sigh,
Does the Grip.
Some maidens fear to know
If moustaches tickle so.
When their nicest Sunday beau
Has the Grip;
But girls, who doubtful are,
Take our word that a cigar
Will destroy bacteria-r
On the lip.
That’s the tip
Let ’em smoke like dear papa,
With the Grip.
Yet nought a spooney lad
Cares if Popsy’s got it bad—
It is catching, too, begad !
Is the Grip;
No fellow stops to think
When the nectar’s there to drink.
He goes, as quick as wink,
For a sip.
Take our tip—
When her skin is white and pink
’Tis unnatural to shrink
From the Gri p.


The special interest that we have always
taken in commercial undertakings which
have for their chief object the promotion of
the well-being of the working man in Aus-
tralia, must be our excuse to-day for bring-
ing under the notice of our readers the
Citizens’ Life Assurance Co., Limited, of
Sydney, whose annual report has just been
issued, disclosing results which supply
material for the consideration of every
thoughtful man.
Life Assurance is one of those questions
which most people reflect upon, and which
wise men act upon in these days. It was not
always thus, but times have changed. As the
duration of human life is more or less un-
certain, men deem it prudent to make pro-
F. B. FREEHILL, Esq., M.A.,
DIRECTOR.
vision against the unforeseen. A rich man
need not be so anxious about these things,
but a man who is not rich, whose family is
dependant upon his weekly or monthly
salary, has no other means open to provide
for them.
Never in the history of Life Assurance
were men so wide awake to its advantages as
at the present time. The exceedingly rapid
growth of the leading offices in recent years
is sufficient proof that educated men are
beginning to find it one of the best invest,
ments. It has, in this sense, a twofold
advantage. In the first place, Life Assur-
ance, when effected in a first-class office,
receives a high rate of interest for the
money invested, which is not always the case
Dr. J. J. POWER,
DIRECTOR.
in commercial investments —this is the busi-
ness point of view. Then again in the higher
and more rational sense, Life Assurance
makes provision for those dependant upon
the assurers. The tenure of life, even in the
healthiest, is so uncertain that it is morally
incumbent upon a man not rich to make
some provision for his family by the aid of
Life Assurance. It is a foolish notion to
think that there is no use assuring unless
you assure for a good “round sum,” but a
“ little store ’ laid by is better than no pro-
vision at all.
We continually hear a great deal about the
want of thrift in the working classes, but the
results achieved by the Citizens’ Life Assur-
ance Company make it clear beyond ques-
tion that the “masses’ are making great
strides in the direction of providing for the
future, and in no class of the community is
this “making provision forthefut uro” of more
importance than to the “ hewers of wood
and drawers of water,” who are nevertheless
the backbone and sinew of the country. The
great middle or industrial class has, until late
years, been practically debarred from avail-
ing themselves of
the benefits of Life
Assurance in the
Australian Colonies
in consequence of
the Assurance Com-
panies charging
their premiums
yearly, half-yearly,
or quarterly, but
whatever 1 excuse
there may have been
in the past, there
is not now a single
reason why every
man, woman, and
child should not
avitil themselves
of the inducements
held out by Life
Assurance Offices.
The Citizens’ de-
vote their special
attention to that
system of Life
Assurance known as
the “Industrial,”
and have framed
WILLIAM JOHN LYNE, Esq., M.P.,
DIRECTOR.
(Late Minister for Lands.)
tables of rates calculated for the weekly
payment of premiums, and issue policies for
sums ranging from £lO upwards. Policies
on the lives of children are issued from date
of birth. Payments can be made from Id. a
week upwards, and the sum assured may be
payable on
death or oil
attaining a giv en
age. Premiums
can be paid
weekly, fort-
nightly or
monthly to suit
the Convenience
of the policy-
holders, and the
agents of the
Company call
periodically at
the houses of
the assured for
the payment of
sums due.
The number
of new policies
issued during
1889 was 67,325,
which far and
atvay excels
anything ever
done in Indus-
trial Life Assur-
ance in this
country before.
The average
amount of the

(Late Colonial Treasurer.)
weekly premiums on the policies in force
on the books of this Company amounts to a
little over 4d. per week, and the amount
of labour involved in the collection of over
£*50,000 must be
enormous, but,
nevertheless, it does
not in any way les-

adopted. Every

In any commer-
S. H. HYAM, Esq., J.P., Ex-M.P.,
DIRECTOR.
(Late Mayor of Balmain.)
cial transaction if any man were to offer to
pay any debt owing by such a small in-
stalment as 4d. per week, the creditor,
sooner than bo put to the trouble of
sending round to have that collected, would
almost prefer to make his debtor a present
of the amount,. It is a sum of not sufficient
magnitude U* attain to the importance of
of an investment, but, if we may so use
the term, it is a sum of money won from the
follies of the people and invested for
them. Small as is that sum let us make
a summary of the labour involved before the
contract is entered
into. The canvasser
of the Company,
often in wet and
slush, travels
throughout the day
seeking everyone
whom he thinks will
probably assure in
his office, and the
successfulcanvasser
lias to bring to

action of his work,
an amount of deter-
mination which is
only characteristic
of a brave soldier
in a good cause.
He has, in addition,
by reason of the
difficulties and pre-

in the minds of
many persons, to
use a skill which
would adorn the
greatest lawyer on
the bench. Then, before the proposal is

be referred to, because the character
and habits of the person must be of that
order and steadiness which would commend

not be enter-
tained in its

The Superin-
tendent or In-
spector has then

the risk, and
if it is of suffi-
cient in agn i-
tude,the Doctor
must make an

The collector has

after week for

money (which,

to the person

of the Company,

with a Company,
whoso system has

The Citizens’ has
branches in all the

agencies in every

land, the business
is under the

Joseph Butler; in

some considerable time, arrangements have
been completed for the establishment of a
fully equipped branch, and it is pleasing to
note that the Company has not boon in the
slightest degree deterred in their oilward
march by the recently passed West Australian
Act, requiring a deposit, with the Colonial
Treasurer there, of £lO,OOO, and it was
announced at the annual meeting that that
deposit will be promptly made. We ob-
serve that similar deposits have been made
in those colonies whose enactments require
such security, and the Managing Director, in
his annual speech, spoke strongly and em-
phatically in favour of the Government of the
Mother Colony, New South W r ales, rising
equal to the occasion and protecting one of
the most sacred interests of the public, by
legislating on Life Assurance according to
modem ideas, and requiring a substantial de-
posit from any Company coming here to

JOHN SEE, Esq., M.P.,

(Late Postmaster General.)
The Citizens’ Life Assurance Company
has indeed earned for itself a reputation
which stands high in the estimation of the
Australian public for the prompt payment of
claims. Out of the large number of claims
paid, the Company has never yet gone to law-
over a single claim, but, on the contrary,,
has always made it a special feature to pay-
all claims immediately after receipt of thu
necessary proofs, without putting claim-
ants to any expense whatever beyond a fee
of 2s. paid to the Registrar of deaths for the
necessary certiticate. The Directors have in
consequence received numerous letters oi
thanks both through the press and privately
from persons who have been entitled to and'
have received prompt payment of claims. A
PRANK COFFEE, Esq.,
DIRECTOR.
list containing copies of some of the letters
received and the names of hundreds of
claimants may be had at any of the Com-
pany’s branch offices, or at the head office.
It may not be out of place here to mention
one or two illustrations of this commendable
promptitude. Our readers will remember
the terrible mining accident which occurred
recently at the A. A. Company’s mines, New-
castle. As a large number of the miners in
the Newcastle district are policy-holders in
the Citizens’, the Directors immediately die
patched one of their officers from the head
office to the scene of disaster, armed with fuh
authority to settle all claims immediately at
they arose. A claim under Policy No.
9319 on the fife of one of the unfortunate
miners, Hodson, was paid to his widow
long before the body was actually ic-
covered. In the recent Hoods in Queens-
land, at Ipswich, Henry Robertson was

day morning and he was drowned on the after-

were paid the full sum assured. Another case
in Queensland —a father jumped down a well
to rescue his child and was himself drowned,
and although his policy had been very re-
cently issued the amount assured was paid in
full. Another policy-holder named Carter,
while bathing in Sydney harbour, was attacked
and devoured by a shark. The claim for the
full amount was paid immediately. In Gee-

poisoned. The poison was in the house, and
during the night, mistaking it for another
fluid, she drank it, and thus a claim arose,

In every phase of society it will th is be
seen that death is constantly occurring, and
in these humble homes nothing could be
more beneficial than a policy taken out in an
office such as this.
The Railway Commissioners of this colony
are now insisting on every official in the
employ of the Department assuring his life,
and they take upon themselves the duty of
naming the offices in which employes may
take out policies. It will be seen from the
following letter that the Citizens' Assurance
Company has been approved of as one of the
Companies for the assurance of railway em-
ployes :
Government Railways of N.S. Wales,
Secretary’s Office, Sydney,
February 26, 1890.
Sir, —In reply to your letter of February 24, I
have the honor to inform you that the Railway
Commissioners have approved of your Society as
one with which employes may insure their lives.

Though chiefly devoting their energies to-
wards the promotion of their Industrial De-
partment, the Citizens’ also transact Ordinary
Life Assurance, and have their Ordinary De-
partment divided into two sections, General
arid Temperance. In the Temperance
section, total abstainers are grouped together,
and any benefit accruing to them from their
total abstinence will be secured to them in
the shape of increased bonuses.
During the whole history of the office, the
management havingalways recognised thefact
that there is ample scope for all, have never
found it necessary to adopt some of the
tactics resorted to by smaller and less suc-
cessful offices in their attempts to secure
business. Comparisons are at all times
odious, and we often regret to notice the
managers of L fe offices, issuing comparative
statements purporting to show the superiority
of their office over others, while the matters
compared are not alike. It would be mani-
festly unfair to compare the working expenses
of an office in its early stages, engaged in
extending its
agencies
throughout the
country, with
those of an old
office, whose
agencies have
been established
for some time
and who are
now engaged in
consolidating
their business.
It would also
be very unfair
to compare the
many points of
the working of
an Industrial
Office with that
of an ordinary
office, but com-
pared with the
results achieved
by offices in
England, trans-
acting a similar
class of business,
the Citizens’ in
many respects
stands in the
front rank.
There is recorded in “ Bourne’s Insurance
Directory” tin proceedings and business
done by every office of importance in England,
Ireland, and Scotland, there being given
returns from 18 of the largest Industrial
Life Offices—although all of them are not
known as Life Offices, some being called
collecting Friendly Societies, but, standing
on the same footing, are so dealt with by
Assurance authorities at Home. From these
• returns we find that for the past year the
Citizens’ takes high rank, and is not by any
means the least. The Citizens’ stands higher
in the list in actual income than eight and
less than ten. But, in the amount added to
funds, there are only six above the Citizens’,
which is superior to twelve ; and, in per-
centage of income saved —a most important
point—the Citizens’ stands at the very top of
the tree.
Assurance, like any other commodity,
might be had too cheap, and it shows con-
siderable foresight on the part of the Direc
tors of the Citizens’, that they charge
reasonable rates for the benefits which they
give, and sensible men will not forget the
fact that if they want a good article they
must pay for it.
To conduct an Assurance office on strictly
fair and straightforward lines, requires the
aid not only of shrewd business men, but
also of professional men—men who, from
their social and commercial position, are not
likely to connect themselves with anythin
shady. The result has been that the Com-
panies, whose aims and objects have secured
for them the services of such men, have
gained for themselves the confidence of the
public, and attained success accordingly.
One such company is the Citizens’, of
which, as an institution of purely Australian
origin, we feel proud. In this issue we give
portraits of the directors and chief officials.
The directors are well-known citizens, whose
faces are no doubt familiar to many of our
readers:—Mr. J. P. Gar van, M.P. (managing
director), and Messrs. W. J. Lyne, M.P.,
John See, M.P., Frank Coffee, F. B, Free-
hill, M. A,, S. H. Hyam, J.P., Dr. Power,
directors ; Win. Alexr. Smith, secretary ;
Dr. JM. .1. Clime, chief medical officer, and
(tie branch officials already named.
A man’s duly is to take care of liis
family, as, according to natural law, he is the
breadwinner. The one disturbing power is
Mr. W. NIMMO DEWAR,
RESIDENT SECRETARY, VICTORIA.
the danger of the accidents that attach them
selves to all things human, whereby the
breadwinner may no longer be able to do his
work, which then would devolve upon the
remaining members of the family. How ill
fitted the untrained wife and the inexperi-
enced children are for that kind of work may
be seen any day in any communiiy of the
land, and the man who lias a proper affection
for his fall lily will seek to prevent that trial
by every means in his power.
The chief disturbing accident or possibility
in human life is death ; and just as a man
would use ’every possible means to prevent
the death of his dear ones, so he should
strive to protect them from the evil results
that would flow from his own death.
Me. JOSEPH BUTLER,
RESIDENT SECRETARY, QUEENSLAND.
A man’s first duty, therefore, is to insure
his life in some responsible company, and
thus make sure the future existence and use-
fulness of the home that he has built up with
the power of his brain and hand.
Any of our readers who contemplate
affecting a life assurance should, before
definitely deciding, send for Citizens’ pros-
pectus and see for themselves. The office
is in every way deserving of the support
which has been accorded it by the working-
chasses throughout the whole of Australia,
and from the good work it is doing it may,
without doubt, be emphatically described as
the “Workingman’s Friend.”

Before Alfred Deakin took to politics he
ivas a leader-writer for the Melbourne Age. A
well-known Irish wit, meeting the editor of that
journal one day, airily remarked : “ I say, you
ought to have something more than a Deacon in
your office —something higher—you ought to have
i bishop on your staff. ” “A bishop! \Vhy so?”
•‘Because your statements are so sadly in need of
xmfirmation.” — Pall Mall.
Sir George Stephen, father of the late
Judge WilberfOrce Stephen (Vic.), and himself in
his day one of the best known of down South
public men, was the author of two books-—the
first a volume of “Hints on Horse dealing the
second a “Life of Christ.” Of the first, two thou-
sand copies were ,sold; of the other, ten copies
only. This discrepancy would seem to imply that
people were tolerably sure of their way to Heaven,
but doubtful of their capacity as horse-coupers.
The Rev. Charles Price, of Launceston
(Tas.), who still flourisheth within a short cooee of
a century, was the last man to shake Johnny
Fawkner’s hand as he stepped from Launceston
old wharf into the boat which was to carry him to
the schooner Enterprise, lying out in the stream
ready for that trip which founded Melbourne.
For several score of years the old gentleman kept
a school at which, among others, many a man
now well-known in Sydney was put into the way
he should go—but didn’t.
Railway Commissioner Eddy, of N.S.W.?
is taking criminal libel proceedings against som e
of his press critics, or, as he prefers to term therm
the slanderers who have been maligning him.
The allegation which most riles Mr. Eddy is that
he has been guilty of many acts of corrupt nepot-
ism ; this he strenuously denies, and it is said to be
mainly with the object of forever disposing of it
that he now takes proceedings. He claims to
have endured with great patience numerous press
attacks, but, finding that some of his enemies (the
names of whom The Bulletin could guess in three
tries) have gone to the expense and trouble of com-
piling a pamphlet full of libels and distributing it
wholesale by post, he has determined to come
right out of his shell and deal with the matter in
one act. The other side of the story, whatever it
may be, will no doubt be set forth in court. It is
no secret that theN.S.W. Railway Commissioners
have incurred much enmity by the extensive and,
it is alleged, unjustifiable scale on which they
have been,importing officers_ from England to dis-
place local men of long service.
“Major” Isaacs, who represents Wal-
worth in the Commons, had the cheek to address
a semi-religious gathering in his constituency
while the charge of intimidation brought by him
against his mistress, whom he is alleged to have
originally seduced, was pending. Isaacs is a
paunchy, bald-headed person with a bill discount-
ing air, and the woman is a thin-lipped, fair, cold-
featured woman who, in court, was at her best
when she looked loftily at her egg-skulled lover,
and was dressed in the style a rich vulgar man
might have been expected to admire.

George Helle, a sober German plumber?
married, and the father of three children, a good
husband and affectionate family man, hanged
kimself the other day in Melbourne, because he
had been live months out of work. He had borne
his troubles with apparent philosophy and had
never outwardly complained. Before killing him-
self he turned the face of his wife’s portrait to the
wall and pat two mirrors “in mourning.” Then
he strangled himself. What is hell ? Hell is to
love your family and see them hungry.”

The Gentiles have been triumphant in the
Salt Lake City elections. It monogamy becomes
law there, what a heap of grass-widows will be
floating round in search rf engagement! Joaquin
Miller, who has been visiting Salt Lake City,
says : “If you put polygamy away the average
Mormon is a mighty g> od Christian —a better
Christian in almost every way than the average
Christian, so called. Xow, this is the truth. I
know of no Christian, rich or poor, who is willing
to fast every 30 days in order to give all his food
of that day to the 'poor. Yet every Mormon does
this voluntarily and continually. 1 know of no
Christian, although I do know of some Jews, who
gives one-tenth of his income to his church. Yet
every Mormon does this continually.” M hich
reminds us that a bill for the institution of poly-
gamy is seriously mooted in Tasmania, the y- ung
men of which fan’ country, being unable to obtain
local unployment, emigrate to Australia, leaving
I their sisters behind them. The Bulletin sees no
j serious social objection to the scheme, for if a
I girl can’t get a whole husband she surely should
jbe allowed to secure a fracti nof one. However,
there might be some difficulty in getting the royal
I assent to such an advanced measure—although
' Tasmania has had at least one Governor who
i passed a private polygamy aet for his own benefit.

NTIPODEAN CLIMATE.
MIDWINTER IN JUNE, MIDSUMMER IN DECEMBER,
HAVE THEIR BANE AND ANTIDOTE.
The climate of this Australasian continent of
ours, taken as a whole, is well suited to the Euro-
peans ; it is remarkably healthy, saving such com-
plaints as are not generally fatal in their tenden-
cies, and which in a measure are more or less com-
mon to all climates. Our climate is peculiar in
some respects ; it is usually very dry, and subject
in summer to hot winds and periodical droughts,
and in winter to heavy rains. The seasons are the
reverse to those in England—December being mid-
summer and June midwinter. But these condi-
tions of temperature are in a physical sense provo-
cative of nerve troubles from the long spells of dry
weather which merge into a long continuance of
wet. In consequence, among the minor ills which
affect the flesh, we have neuralgia, but the bane
has its antidote, as the experiences which tollow,
will show Mr. Alf. Hay, of Boomanoomana,
N.S.W., suffered at one time terribly with neu-
ralgia, and was cured by St. Jacobs oil. Second
Officer Win. H. Hawes, P.M.S.S. “City of New
York,” says : “I obtained astonishing relief from
acute neuralgia of the face by using St. Jacobs
oil.” Mr. W. H. Mathieson, the well-known
coachbuilder, of Dee-street, Invercargill, N.Z.
says: “An apprentice of mine was taken ill with
neuralgia, and became delirious. He found relief
from one application of St. Jacobs oil.” Mr.
William Walker (Walker, Lemon & Co.), 12
Lonsdale-street, Melbourne writes: “I suffered
from a severe attack of neuralgia—pain intense—
and after two applications of St. Jacobs oil the
pain ceased magically, and has never returned.”
The death rate of the Australasian colonies for
1830 was as follows New Zealand 11.46, Western
Australia 13.24, Queensland 13.59, Victoria 13.70,
South Australia 14.85, New South Wales 15.57,
Tasmania 16.12. The mean of the colonies was
14.01. In England and Wales the rate on an
average of 30 years appears to be 22.3.
MASTER MARINER’S OPINION.
The popular Commander of s.s. Iberia writes as
follows to the Adelaide agent of the Waterbury
Watch Co. ;
“Pacific Steam Navigation Company’s
“ R.M.S. Iberia,
“ Adelaide, 12th March, 1890.
“Dear Sir, —I beg to state that I have carried
with me during the past two years one of the
short-winding Waterbury watches. It has proved
a most reliable timekeeper, and I find it most useful
at sea, as moving the hands about does not seem
to affect its accuracy.
“ 1 remain dear sir, yours truly,
“Wm. Waddilovk, Lieut. R.N.R., Commander,
“ per Thos. Kidd.”

“ Tinnenburra ” : Roma is an incorporated borough and
not a city. “ City ” (in early days most cities were re-
publics), civilly considered, implies self-rule ; ecclesiasti-
cally regarded, the see of a bishop. In some of the
colonies Parliamentary consent is required ere a town can
become a city. A town is a numerous assemblage of
houses incorporated as a municipality. Australian con-
ceptions of the words differ from both English and
American... “Reader” (Tas.); About six millions ...

THE WAY THE MONEY GOES.
The Anheuser Busch Brewing Association em-
ploys 2200 men at its brewery in St. Louis. It is
customary to allow all employes a liberal supply
of the famous St. Louis Lager Beer for their own
consumption while at work, the beer being drawn
from packages ready for market. The beer thus
consumed amounts in value to £3O per day, and
sometimes reaches £4O, or about £lO,OOO a year.

1. Winter, an "Australian native” of
Richmond (Vic.), wants to go and explore the
South Pole. No go; the South Pole has its own
universal winter.
Governor Carington says he is going to
stay out his full Australian term of office, and
that it is untrue, us alleged, that he has already
taken some flats in London. The latter rumour
The Bulletin believes to be based on the fact
that his Ex. has been taken by a lot of *’ flats in
Australia, who rent him for about £15,000 a year.
If you would have a thorough change from
the country, you cannot do better than secure
rooms at the Grand Pier Hotel, Manly, which has
been entirely re-furnished, and is now being con-
ducted in first-class style. Its location is one of
the most beautiful anywhere, and the attractions
include a perfect cuisine and unchallengeable
wines and liquors, unrivalled sea-bathing, enjoy-
able steamer-trips up and down Sydney Harbour,
the most lovely drives in Australia, and excellent
fishing down the coast.
Now that the Lochs have gone, one or two
little stories are creeping into the ears of the
vulgar public that would indicate that all was not
so nice as it seemed between them and Premier
Gillies—-doubtless owing to Lady Loch's belief
that no one not born an aristocrat should have any
peceadilloes of the Don .) uanesque order. One of
the latest stories is that Kahler was given sittings
for his picture of “ The Lawn ”by both the Gov.
and Gillies. When His Ex. saw the portrait of
the latter, he said: “Haw, not unlike, but not
voluptuous enough.” Gillies got home with:
“ That the Governor ? Really, 1 should not have
recognised him. You have made him too hand-
some and intellectual-looking ! ” Wfih'cli left
neither top dog.
Stanley has a rare knack for conceiving
situations—he is a born bookmaker. He is nearly
as good as Parkes at making history. At the
execution of the ringleader of the mutiny against
the departure of Emin Pasha, he worked up the
situation in a way worthy of Handsome George.
First, Stanley is sick, and has, “ by the force of
his determined will ” (which phrase is pretty well
threadbare), to be carried outside his tent, and
tints addresses the wretched culprit : “ We have
come thousands of miles, through thousands
of dangers, to rescue you, and this is our
reward. Go to God ” (not to hell, mind yon,
as a common Australian would have said.)
Naturally this was rather vague, and the
crowd around wanted to know the means of
i transit to the celestial presence. With lean figure
the great explorer points to an overhanging bough
and the prisoner. “Send him to God,” lie says,
and by means of a rope lie is sent there ! Turn on
the limelight. If this is darkest Africa how are we
going to set an example of civilisation? Why, the
old P.M. up North who told the police-sergeant re
the drunk to “take him out and hang him”
comes out just as well.
The brother-in-law of a murderer lately
hanged at Wagga (N.S.W.) has just committed a
murder in Tasmania. Another conclusive proot
of the efficacy of capital punishment.
In Thebes lately the Egyptologists found
a papyrus 70 feet long, and 15 inches broad. It is
beautifully illuminated, and contains hieroglyphic
extracts from the famous and mysterious book of
theUead. The whole is to be photographed. Copies
will be obtainable by the Sydney and Melbourne
Public Libraries, In fact, we think they are to
be got now, if the authorities only bestir them*
selves. The papyrus is about 4,000 years|old.
The “Colonial Year Book, 1890,” which
has been fulsoincly praised by the London press, is
a not-too-intelligently-done rehash of out-of-date
information originally supplied from official
sources.” In the section of New South Wales, as
regards the past, Flanagan’s spurious version of
Governor Phillip’s inaugural oration is quoted as
authentic; and as to the present, anyone not
knowing better would gain the impression that
the colony has a State religion—Anglicanism.
A long, bony Melbourne larrikin, who
probably felt tired of life, spotted Prof. William
Miller walking down Bounce street with a lady
one day last week, whereupon an uncontrollable
impulse to “shove up against ’ William took
possession of this misguided wretch. Four times
did the Professor earnestly, yet softly, request
the long object to leave off shoving and depart in
peace—in one piece. The. lady also expressed a
hope that they would take him away before he
was killed, but the gathering crowd just grinned
and waited for the blow to fall. It was after the
fifth shove that the blow fell. The Professor’s
“ right made a strategic movement, a sound like
the cracking of a Brazil nut in a front door was
heard above the city’s din, and the larrikin lay
stretched across the tram-line as Miller went
marching on. The Professor had knocked his
persecutor about 36 feet at one fell swoop.

Says London Echo :
lu “Tasma,”authoress of “ Uncle Piper ot Piper's Hill”
wc have one of the two or three precursors of Australian
literature. It is evident that the hand which wrote
“ Uncle Piper” is capable of original work of a very high
order. Australia appears to he far more precocious in the
production of a truly native literature than any other
English-Speaking land outside the old country. Of
American literature, it could have been said, for genera-
tions, that it might as well have been written iu the
Mother Country. One reason for this distinction is that
the Australian ideal of life is far less materialistic than
that of America.
The rowing gaffers of Sydney are gradually
being cornered by O’Connor. The latter lias only
to be watched in one regard ; he must not be per-
mitted to scoot to Yankceland after deposing a
false Australian champion. A big sweepstake
would best settle the championship question.

If Sir Alfred Stephen publishes in his
autobiography all he knows about “ our first fami-
lies,” what an interesting volume it will be ! The
man who was Crown Solicitor in Van Dieman’s
Land 65 years ago, can a tale unfold respecting
the ancestors of “ our landed gentry.”
The plans and elevation of the new Argus
office, Melbourne, show a comparatively modest
structure, of three stories. There is no pretence
of show, the sole consideration being adaptability.
People thought there would be some emulation of
the Tribune, Herald, and World buildings, in
New York, but the fact is a newspaper never can
fill up such places with its own work.
Mrs. Harrison Lee, a young lady of Rich-
mond, Melbourne, announces the publication of a
new book on “Marriage and the Social Evil.”
This will largely expand some views she has ex-
pounded both in the religious and secular press of
Melbourne; but the editors, in both branches, got
frightened and put the brake on, Mrs. Lee is
prominent in the religious world, and will outdo
Mona Caird.
Many years ago, says an English magazine,
when George Francis Train was in Australia, the
captain of an ocean steamship was fined heavily in
court for ejecting on his latest voyage six male
passengers from the ladies’ saloon of the steamer,
the rules forbidding their entering that cabin. He
had not enough cash. Could not raise it. Could
not appeal and the delay in sailing would ruin him.
Train, who was a spectator, stepped forward and
got permission of the court to advise the captain
in private. After a lapse of half-an-hour he re-
turned, and was about to address the court, when
one of the magistrates asked where the captain
was. “I don’t know,” said Train, “he is no
client of mine. I left him outside.” Search was
made and a line of black smoke in the offing
showed that the captain was off for England.
Edison has developed his idea of using the
photograph ip conjunction with the phonograph
to register a speaker’s personality.—
In front of the speaker are placed two small machines,
one a phonograph and the other an ingenious piece of
mechanism by which photographs of the speaker are
taken in succession with enormous rapidity at intervals of
from one-eighth to one-twentieth of a second, and both
these machines are at work silently recording both the
uttered speech and the personal appearance of the speaker
at each fractional part of a second. The results thus ob-
tained may be sent to any desired point and the photo-
graphs thrown on a screen by an ingeniously-contrived
piece of mechanism. Thus the exact appearance of a
speaker, with all his gestures and play of features, is
exactly reproduced, while the phonograph simultaneously
delivers the speech. The interval between the successive
photographs is so infinitesimal that the screen picture is
apparently a living one, moving, gesticulating, and appar-
ently uttering the words which in fact are spoken by the
phonograph. The greatest difficulty experienced by
Edison in his experiments was the synchronization of the
two instruments, so that the utterances of the phonograph
should exactly coincide with the gesticulations; but this
was finally overcome, and the experiments were crowned
with perfect success.

The Grand Duchess of Baden says she can
never forget that her late mother, the Empress
Augusta, told her that—“A princess must never
be tired.” They have no reason to be ; it is the
people who support them who get tired.
A London amateur boxer, having been
killed by compression of the brain—through the
rupture of small blood-vessels in the surface of its
covering caused by concussion following on blows —
an inquest was held. Coroner : “Then every time
a man is ‘ knocked out ’ or becomes insensible he
is in danger?” Surgeon : “ Certainly he is.” Larry
Foley and the Gymnastic Club committees should
paste this in their hats, unless they hanker after
manslaughter prosecutions,
A Lancashire bridegroom was lately mar-
ried on a drizzling Sunday morning, and, there
being no wedding-breakfast, the parson detained
the party for the morning service. At its con-
clusion the bridegroom, who had been looking
very melancholy, said to a friend, “Oi wouldn’t
hev corned at all if Oi’d known it was going to be
so glum.” And on getting outside the church
and finding that it was raining he took to his heels
and bolted home, leaving his bride to her own
devices.
The Sydney Nation, started “to advo-
cate the claims and maintain the rights of the
Irish Catholic minority of N.S.W. and Austra-
lasia generally,” has suspended publication, “ hav-
ing once again been deceived by hundreds on
hundreds of definite pledges of support —the
pledges of self-styled subscribers not too proud to
take in the Nation weekly, yet too mean to hand
over the payments due to us for regularly supply-
ing it to them.” Moral; If you are in Australia
you can’t get a living out of Ireland.
Surely Bismarck is in the right about
Hanover, The Hanoverian king chose to take
the Austrian side against the Prussians in 1866,
and shared in the defeat. Now Queen Victoria,
his relation, has been wheedling the German
Emperor, who is ready, they say, to hand over a
large pile of money to the ex-king in settlement of
imaginary claims. Bismarck says no, and the
nation is with him. Money settles everything
with the House of Hanover. Princess Alice’s
husband, Prince of Hesse, had two gorgeous
uniforms ready, in the Prusso-Austrian war, to
don the colours of the victorious side.
A terrible lot of Lord Mayor’s banquets,
leg-shows, royal flummery, and other expensive
luxuries, seem to have been wasted upon the Shah
of Persia, in London. They laid on a special
telegraph service to his State apartments at
Buckingham Palace, gave him a telephone to play
with, and let him slaughter bullocks on the hearth-
rug. Victoria by the Grace went so far as to
shut her eyes to the fact that some of the Shah’s
“ male ” suite were really Persian girls in disguise.
She sat next to the malodorous heathen, and pre-
tended not to mind a bit. In return for all these
concessions the goggle-eyed monster, it is cabled,
handed over the control of his railways to Russia,
and sits swilling sherbert while the British Lion
totters down Piccadilly, mumbling “ euchred
again ! ” It’s too disgusting !

Editor Pike’s New Departure.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The amours and bitterness of the common,
domestic obituary poem and its attendant jour-
nalistic wickednesses, the birth and marriage
proclamations, which tend to elevate the
soul and burnish up her mental attributes, have
not been entirely exhausted by the gaunt and
soulless human objects who fabricate long links
of irreverent literature for the comic papers,
as has been evidenced by a new phase'of wretched-
ness that has recently appeared in the domestic
column of a small and dreadfully solemn organ
run in the interest of liver-pills and lung-blisters
in a lost and disconsolate township called Cooney-
bungle, South Australia.
The Cooneybungle Bugle is under the dreamy
supervision of a stunted literary individual named.
Pike, who has worn all the nap off his‘head
wrestling with European complications and sup-
porting the awful aggravation and weary respon-
sibilities of propelling and controlling a Weekly
paper with only the assistance of an offensive and
dunder-headed small boy. Mr. Pike is an under-
done and tremendously depressed gehtleman with a
stealthy, sidelong gait, and a face expressive of all
the deeper emotions of a prize Swede turnip; he
devotes a great deal of the time and attention
that should be spent in pushing and furthering
the Bugle to indulgence in beer and other native
products of a kindred nature, and is occasionally
seen spread about in public places in a state of
mental and physical incapacity at moments of
great importance to the well-being of his valuable
journal.
Towards the end of last year a brilliant original
idea, having for its purpose the spread and wider
popularity of the Bugle, occurred to Mr. Pike,
and in an early issue of that brainy and popular
organ an announcement to the effect that the pro-
prietors, always anxious to increase the usefulness
and interest of their paper, would in future, on
the 30th of every December, publish a summary of
all marriages and births advertised in the Bugle
during the past year, also an In Memoriam over
each late-lamented person whose death had been
notified in their columns within the same space of
time. The aforesaid proprietors cunningly intimated
hopes that their enterprise would tend to encour-
age the births, marriages, and deaths in the
neighbourhood, which institutions certainly par-
took of the general depression in business in and
about Cooneybungle. The Bugle, of course, had
only the interest of the township at heart in
making this departure, and was confident the
residents would do their best to second its en-
deavours and make the birth, marriage, and
death-rate of Cooneybungle second to none in
Australia.
The idea evoked immediate expressions of pub-
lic approval; the residents were not expected to
pay anything, and that alone was sufficient to ex-
cite their hearty co-operation. At the instigation
of the local doctor, a meeting of prominent inhabi-
tants was held for the purpose of taking into con-
sideration the best means of expressing their high
esteem for the character and ability of Mr.
Joachim Pike, the editor of the Cooneybungle
Bugle. The doctor, two clergymen, and the
undertaker addressed this meeting, and dwelt in
glowing terms upon the beautiful morality of Mr.
J, Pike and his untiring efforts for the good of the
township. Dr. Kidd instanced the Murphy-street
drain, the post-office lamp and the Burnt Creek
bridge, as lasting monuments of Mr. Pike’s
indefatigable zeal, and remarked that this last
sacrifice in throwing open the already sorely-
pressed columns of the Bugle, with a view to
accelerating the local birth-rate, proved that he
was fully alive to the most pressing necessities of
the township The doctor hoped all present
would unite in giving their able editor a fitting
token of their appreciation. The Rev. Peter Pope
had long been animated by the keenest admiration
for Mr. Joachim Pike’s literary abilities, and felt
that his wonderful penetration in discovering the
infrequency and irreligious reluctance with which
the young people of Cooneybungle contracted
matrimony, and his self-sacrificing endeavours to
ameliorate that evil deserved some return ; and
then the reverend gentleman started off on a
lingering dissertation on the impiety of single life,
and was overtaken by. the chairman an hour, later,
and called off to make room for Brown, the under-
taker. Brown said old Pike was a smart fellow ;
he knew a thing or two ; he could see that some
people remained alive away beyond reason and
religion—lived overtime, so to speak—to the
ruination of business and public morality, and was
not afraid to tell them so. He (Brown) had a
great admiration for the editor of the Bugle, and
would be happy to supply him with a German-
silver • mounted coffin gratis, if he would step
round and leave his measure. The meeting
resolved unanimously to tender Mr. Pike a public
banquet at an early date.
Mr. Pike got his banquet—it was a small,
inexpensive banquet, consisting chiefly of wet
refreshments,-and it was bought and paid for by the
doctor, the undertaker, and the clergymen. But
Joachim was modest in his desires, and manifested
his appreciation of the fare provided by getting as
drunk as time and circumstances would permit.
He was carried home in the morning on an arm-
chair, and returned to consciousness some hours
later, animated with an unconquerable desire to
protract the festivities.
The next number of the Bugle was looked for-
ward to with considerable anxiety by the people of
the township, few of whom had not experienced
either a birth, death, or marriage in their families
during the past twelve months; but when the
paper came out a sudden chill fell upon the
general expectations, and Cooneybungle retired
within itself, abashed, to cogitate and endeavour
to unravel a great waste of tangled-up birth-and-
death proclamations and distracted in-memorial
at the Bugle sufficed to convince its
numerous subscribers that its gifted editor must
have been violently drunk just prior to its publi-
cation ; the promised column looked as if it had
bean struck by lightning and then printed in a
mangle .expressions of howling despair and
bottomless grief were hashed up amongst songs
of rejoicing'and heartfelt congratulations, and all
the most influential gentlemen of Cooneybungle
were, given due, pro vocation to get up in. tumultu-
ous bitterness add kick the enlightened Bike and
welt him with a waddy from Dan to Beersheba.
Whether the aforesaid offensive small boy ha
inadvertently.-knocked the stuffing out of a form
and then filled it again according to the dictate
of his own mildewed conscience, or Joachim, m
one of his fits of vinous aggravation, had set the
matter up regardless of law and order, has never
been made known, but the Bugle’s new departure
was a miserable- failure, and moved large, raus-
cular residents to call upon Mr. 1 ike in his °mee
and express their sentiments and invite that
gentleman ofit'to fight; but they found the guiding
intellect and full staff of their only journal spread
in an attitude of abandon on a pile of exchanges
-,iu a dunib, solid slumber brought on by rum.
Despite kicks and profane expostulations the
editor maintained his somnolent composure, and
his interviewers, each armed with a Bugle, dropped
one by one into the Old Coach Hotel to ventilate
their grievances and invite sympathy. Here an
impromptu public-meeting was held, and Doctor
Kidd w4s voted to the chair.
The doctor was painfully agitated and ruth-
lessly undone; he arose and flourished a Bugle,
and without preliminaries stigmatised Bike as a
debauched reprobate- and an old, bald-headed,
rattletrap scoundrel, with no conscience and no
reverence for the finest emotions that animate the
human bosom.
“You all know,” said the raging medico,
“that early in March I was united in the holy
bonds of wedlock to Mrs. Stretch, t the relict of
your late respected poundkeeper. Hear what
MR. C. W. GOODCHAP.
this low-minded, rum-ravaged animal of the
Bugle has to say of that joyous compact ”
KIW)-STRETCH.— On the Bth of March, at St. John’s
Church, by the Rev. Amos Boland, Dr. William Kidd
to Mrs. Mary Stretch, daughter of Mr. Heflernan
Stretch, of Liverpool, England.
Not many months have sped since he
Was reft of all the joys of life
And laid in that cold, cruel bed ;
But, ah ! tho’ here we suffer strife,
And pain and anguish ring our brows,
We feel it all is for the best,
And in that Paradise above
We yet may know a perfect rest.
“There !” yelled the doctor, growing extremely
red and tearing up the paper he held, “ there’s a
pretty way to speak of one of the happiest unions
ever sanctioned in Heaven ! 111 give him ‘perfect
rest,’the low. brainless outcast! I’ll make him
‘suffer strife,’ the animal—the mean, crawling,
doddering object of abhorrence!”
Here the medico sank gasping into his chair and
feebly mopped the leakage off his bald tract with
a bar towel, and the meeting proceeded in an
informal way.. , ~
The next complainant was a tall, thin, placid
man who begged to state, without heat or preju-
dice,’ that Joachim Pike, editor of the Cooney-
bungle Bugle, was everything low and degraded,
in nature and several supernatural things detest-
able to humanity, and he requested to be allowed
to move a motion that that meeting, without
further consideration, seize upon the person of
that wretched man, dip it in a barrel of tar, and
roll it down a hill overgrown with thistles. He
called the attention of all present to the Bugle s
notice of the birth of his tenth daughter :
MORAN.-On the 11th of June, at Graham-street, the
wife of A. Crawford Moran, of a girl, the only daughter
of the late John Sheehan, of Belfast, Ireland. No
cards. Home papers please copy.
Mr. Moran drew attention to the ambiguity of
this intimation in quiet, but forcible, language,
and said that if Pike could not keep wholly
irrelevant birth and marriage notices from
coalescing in that disgusting manner he was
quite unfit to run a paper, and ought to be
hung to prevent further complications. He ob-
served, moreover, that the utter villainy of the
man was to be seen in his request to the Home
papers to copy the report anrl thus reflect upon
the character of Mrs. Moran in a country where
she had hitherto been regarded with respect and
reverence. , , •
The Rev. Mr. Pope then got up in a state of
rage ill-befitting a man of his cloth and com-
plexion, and x’cad : —.
POPE.—On the 21st of September, at her late residence
, 42 Grant-street, Jane, the only wife of the Rev. 1.
Pope, after a long and severe illness. Both doing
well.”
The reverend gentleman went on to say that
such a notice was enough to convince all thinkmg
men that Editor Pike was an atheist and a scoffer
at all good, human or divine. He solemnly as-
sured all present that there was a place prepared
for the devil and his angels and the man who
could publish a notice like that. To say he was
“doing well ” after the death of his dear wife was
a base and malicious libel, as everybody knew who
had witnessed his grief and seen him weighed
before and since. „
Brown, the undertaker, next took the tloor.
Brown had been married a few months prior, to
the daughter of the local publican, and in the
Bugle the record of their nuptials got mixed up
with the following In Memoriarn, bearing on some
late lamented young lady :
Weep not for me, my parent dear,
Cut off in early bloom
From all I loved and cherished here,
And wedded to the tomb.
Wipe off the briny tear-drops now
That down your dear face run.
Drive wrinkled care from off your brow,
And say—“ God's will be done !”'
Mr. Brown seemed to think that rather
rough on him somehow, and hastened to revoke
the promise of a coffin which he had made to Pike,
and alluded to that literary individual through-
out a long harangue in unvarying terms of bitter-
ness ; he said he was inconceivably abject in mind
and ugly and insanitary in person, and he offered,
should the meeting agree in its wisdom to destroy
the editor, to undertake to bury him with the
utmost ignominy at his own expense.
After these a long list of severely distempered
men and ladies started up to ventilate their
grievances, and lay their cases before the meeting,
and offer suggestions for the harassing of Editor
Pike. One large, fierce female was awful in her
wrath, and thought Pike ought to be boiled
because of the following ;
PURDEY—SHANNON.—On the 12th of March, at the
bride’s residence, by the Rev. P. Pope, John Purdey to
Matilda Shannon. May he R.I.P.
Mrs. Purdey resented the insulting inference in
that “ R.1.P.,” and lamented the fact that
Joachim had no hair by which she might haul him
a mile to drop him down a mine.
A gentleman named Jones used a lot of intem-
perate language and violent gesticulation previous
to reading this to the assembly
JONES—LYNCH.—At St. John’s Church, Cooneybungle,
on the 6th of May, 1889, by the Rev. A. Boland, Ed-
ward Jones, of Graham-street, to Miss Elizabeth
Lynch, second daughter of Barnard Lynch, of Cross
Cut, S.A.
In affectionate remembrance of my beloved wife Helen
who passed away on the 3rd of February, 1889; —
She was a loving wife and true
Whilst here on earth she stayed,
And when above the clouds she flew
And with the angels played
' *n golden harps, my broken heart
To follow her was fain.
Dear wife ’twas hard from thee to part.
But soon we’ll meet again.
Inserted by her sorrowing husband, E. Jones.
This was simply a little over-sight in giving the
notice of Jones’ second marriage in conjunction
with his obituary poem for Mrs. Jones No. 1, but
Jones seemed to consider it a matter of great
gravity, and offered to hang Pike and find the
rope if the meeting would only lend its moral
support.
One man who was young and rash took pas-
sionate exception to the fact that the dates of his
marriage and the birth of his first child were re-
versed, and another tabled this as justification for
his awful wrath
BUCK.—In loving and affectionate memory of her grand-
father, the wife of Mr. William Buck of a son. “The
Lord givoth and the Lord taketh away.”
The rest of the birth-and-marriage notices and
obituaries were all either incoherent blocks of
“pied ” typeor drunken and incapable verse, or con-
tained more or less implied aspersions on the
moral character or mental capacity of the persons
mentioned therein, and the demand for blood rose
to a mania in and about Cooneybungle ; but after
calling Editor Pike everything fearful and dis-
agreeable in the English language and giving dua
deliberation to the case, the meeting resolved no)
to resort to bloodshed, but to appoint a deputation
of eight to wait on the journalist and demand an
explanation and a retractation of everything, and
then to tar him and let him loose.
The deputation appeared at the Bugle estab-
lishment in office hours next morning, thinking to
catch the editor sober when their admonitions
would have due effect, but were grieved to learn
that Mr. Pike had been called away to visit a sick
aunt in Adelaide. The shock-headed, vicious
small boy imparted this information from a top-
story window, and then carelessly spilt a lot of
used-up machine-oil on the deputation of eight
and withdrew.
There was no issue of the Bugle for three
weeks, and when public animosity had got cold
and flaccid, Editor Pike managed to get out an
edition containing a full and complete apology to
everybody mentioned, and a good many who were
not mentioned at all, before the people were aware
of his return, and as yet no murder has been re-
ported from Oooneybungle.
EDWARD DYSON.
Mr. C. W. Goodchap.
Charles William Goodchap, architect, of 184
Pitt-street, Sydney, whose likeness appeam else-
where, is a nephew of the well-known Mr. 0. A.
Goodchap, M.L.A. Mr. C. W. Goodchap, whose
father was the District Civil Engineer at Kurnool,
Madras, is a native of India, and was educated
under the Rev.. Thom, at Doveton College,
Madras, Bishop Cotton’s Grammar school,
Bangalore, and at Hanwell College, England, and
the private college of the Rev. Dr. Swam,
M.A., and was then articled to Charles Goodman
Mumby, the well-known London architect. Mr.
Goodchap afterwards increased his experience by
working under Architects Thomas Durrans and
Sir Charles Street on the magnificent New Law
Courts and on the Metropolitan Board of Works
under Mr. Valliamy, chief architect to the Board.
He then began to practise for himself in Queen
Victoria-street, London, where he was very sue-
cessful until ill-health impelled him to visit Aus-
tralia. On arrival Mr. Goodchap, who holds a
certificate for drawing from the South Kensing-
ton Institute, and is a member of the_ Architects
Association, received an appointment in the office
of the Colonial Architect, Sydney, but resigned
his office to enter business on his own account 4i
years ago. He has since been largely engaged in
surveying and architectural designing, and among
his more important works may be enumerated the
branch offices of the City Bank at Kiama, Goota-
mundra, Young and Armidale, buildings costing
considerably over £4,000 in each instance. Many
private buildings in the neighbourhood of Sydney
are the products of Mr. Goodchap’s skill and
varied experience.
Queen Victoria showed her contempt for
Parliament not only by refusing to deliver the
opening speech, but by going to Osborne while it
was being drafted, thereby causing some members
of the Cabinet to make several trips across the
Solent.
The Melbourne University authorities,
presumably on the grounds of modesty, have pro-
vided the lady-students with a separate dissecting-
room, and now the students want to know if the
lambs should not be also drafted from the goats
in hospital surgical practice. Pending develop-
ments, “some of the senior male, gtudents have
expressed their intention of absenting themselves
from all operations at which women are present.”
Your average young woman is, whether from
innocence, ignorance or mental differentiation, far
less modest —or shall we say bashful than your
average young man, but a course of the ballet soon
makes the latter callous, while the former gets, as
a rule, more aggressively modest in male society
with single-agedness.
Stuart Cumberland writes that on one
occasion, while staying at Petty s Hotel, Sydney,
Sir Arthur Gordon (the hideous caricature of
Royalty who used on occasion to make himself
ridiculous by wearing a State-train held up
by pages) entered the drawing-room in which his
wife and some other ladies were seated. Lady
Gordon at once rose up, and it was not until her
husband had left the room that she resumed her
seat. “ Why did you get up like that ?” asked a
lady friend. “Oh, Sir Arthur expects it” was
the reply. “He may expect it at Goyernmcnt
House, but here, in a public hotel, the thing is too
absurd,” rejoined the lady, with considerable m-
“We Australians don t nnrjer&tand
such nonsense.” Now why on earth should a man
expect his wife to arise in his presence because he
lives at Government House? That Australian
woman must have been a lovely snob.

Gladstone
In view of the blood vainly shed on Majuba Hill, of the
noble Irish, Scotch, and English lives wrecked amid the
deserts of the Soudan ; in view of the immolation of those
splendid Arabs who fought so devotedly for their country
and their faith ; in view, above all, of the sacrifice of the
heroic Gordon at Khartoum-sent out by Mr Gladstone
to suit his own purposes, and then abandoned to bis fate
—in view of all this I express not only my profound con-
viction, but the conviction of many sober-minded men,
when I say that this English statesman, judged oo-
iectivelv, and in reference to acts brought about, not,
perhaps by his intention, but by his mismanagement,

The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
The Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literary
Journal, is the only paper which has a con-
sistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived diiectly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces 4^
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in. The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a Vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for high-class printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form—printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aub
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for yours it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its iuccption.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-fire
of national progress : it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
■ wealth, power, and privilege—the direct cause of
half the misery which now afilicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exorcise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised. The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from' the Columns of a daily paper
issued from the same otlice. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Buli.etin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin's red cover is equally familial
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-shcllor of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Now Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of the
ntelligent bushman must indeed be broadly bated.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more than a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed but-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The
Bulletin’s popularity is found in the unchallenge-
able fact—easily ascertainable from any postal
official—that,’of all journals issued in Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mail cd from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonics.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its .vast mass of original and
Specially- written reading-matter is-the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public. . . •

IJHE EQUITABLE LIFE ASSURANCE
Society of the United States.
ASSETS ' ~ ~ Ti I £21,800,000
ANNUAL INCOME ... « £6,250,000
SURPLUS .. .. .. £5,600,000
The Equitable’s record for the year 1889 in
every point of view presents the most extraordinary
exhibit in the history of life assurance. It sur-
passes that of every other Life Company extant.
Some idea may be formed of the magnitude of
the invested funds of the Equitable Life Assur-
ance Society from the fact that they exceed the
combined funds of the eleven Australian Life
Companies, as given in the January number of
“The Australasian Insurance and Banking Re-
cord,” by more than £7,250,000 sterling.
Australian Branches.
It is now about six years since the Equitable
Life Assurance Society of the United States com-
menced business in Australia, and during this
period it has issued in Australasia a larger amount
of Assurance than any other Life Office has done
in the Australian colonies during the first six
years of its existence in these colonies.
The establishment here of the Equitable Life
Assurance Society marks an epoch in the practice
of Life Assurance in these colonies, and has been
the means of introducing many reforms in the
practice of the Australian Life Offices.
The confidence and patronage of the public are
the more gratifying when it is borne in mind that
.from the advent of the Equitable in Australia the
managers of some of the local offices have, in a
spirit much to be deprecated amongst Life Assur-
ance Companies, adopted a tone of hostility
towards the Equitable.
Reforms. /
The Equitable has ever been foremost in intro-
ducing and adopting reforms tending to the
security, comfort, and convenience of its policy-
holders. Founded under a most strict and severe
form of Government supervision, it has, neverthe-
less, from time to time invited the most searching
investigation by independent actuaries and valua-
tors unconnected with the staff of the society;
and such examinations have greatly assisted the
Equitable in prosecuting its business, as its great
financial strength has been thereby authoritatively
established. The good management of the Equit-
able, no less than its strength, is conclusively
shown by the fact that its assets exceed its liabili-
ties by more than 27 per cent.
Protection of Policy Holders.
The Equitable Life Assurance Society has ad
voeated, and will at any time welcome any fair
and uniform legislation in Australia WHICH'
WILL EFFECTUALLY PROTECT THE
INTERESTS OF ALL POLICY-HOLDERS;
WHETHER THEY ARE ASSURED IN
LOCAL OR, FOREIGN OFFICES. Will tfie
local companies cordially assist in setting up the
strict and uniform standard of valuation which
would be required for this purpose ?
Valuation Standard.
So far, no local office has advocated the compul-
sory use by all companies of a uniform pure
premium standard of valuation, based upon the
Actuaries’ rate of mortality and 4 per cent, rate
of interest. Without some such uniform standard
it would be impossible to determine the sufficiency
of the assets held by any company to meet its
liabilities.
Unequalled Strength.
The Equitable Life Assurance Soeiety of the
United States has more assurance in force than
any other Life Company in the world. It transacts
more new business. It has a larger surplus,
measured by the strictest standard of valuation.
Its organisation in Australia gives to the assured
all the advantages of a local office, and, what is
more, the security afforded by the strongest Life
Office in the world.

“Millionaire” Abraham Biggs, of Haw-
thorn, Melbourne, died the other day. His will
has been proved at only £1(30,000. He was a great
friend of the Churches, and was always ready to
assist them —with his valuable advice.
From a recent leading article in the
Times, and endorsed by Lord Brassey :
We are proud of being partners in a business of dimen-
sions without parallel and difficult to realise, and iw are
resolve i to keep whole and undivided the family that has
created it, and in whose hands it is desirable that the
business should remain.
I he italics are ours, and signify that there’s going
to be a fight by-and-bye.
Onofroff, a new thought-reader, is finding
pins at one of the London theatres. He was
green enough to try experiments on our old friend,
Stuart Cumberland, but was not successful with
the rival showman. When Cumberland pretended
to think about one thing he probably concentrated
his mighty mind on something else, thereby bring-
ing poor Onofroff to confusion. Which somehow
reminds us of a schoolboys’ game called “ Buck,
buck.” One boy sits on another boy’s back and
says, “Buck, buck, how many fingers do I hold
up?” When the buck guesses right the rider
generally says he guessed wrong, and so the game
goes on until the buck collapses from exhaustion.
Whether the buck is a good thought-reader or not
doesn’t make the slightest difference to the astute
boy on top.
A South Australian jury recently brought
in the following verdict: —
We are of opinion that the fire... was caused by
some person or persons unknown, but there is no evidence
to show how it originated.
Mr. Frank Guest, of Waverley, one of
John Shepherd’s chief supporters at Paddington
parliamentary election, attended one night during
the last N.S.W. “general” a political meeting at
which Sir Henry Parkes spoke in support of Mr.
Shepherd, who, seeing the Foreign-trade chieftain
appear very fagged, told Guest to get a bottle of
champagne for the old man. Guest, who also felt
fagged, at once sent out for a large bottle of fizz,
calculating that it contained enough for two. On
the arrival of the wine Guest took Sir Henry into
an ante-room, opened the bottle and filled H.P.’s
man’s glass. Then he filled it again, and again, and
again, and then twice more, and then the Freetrade
idol went out and talked very hard. A year after-
wards Mr. Guest, who had first met Sir Henry
under the circumstances just set forth, and who
meanwhile had been chosen as Mayor of Waverley,
waited on him as one of a deputation. A word
with you, Mr. Mayor,” said Sir Henry as the
deputation was retiring—“ I ’ad you very nicely on
the occasion of our ♦‘rst hinteryiew.” “How’s
that?” inquired Mr. Mayor. “ Ah,” said Parkes,
in his little treble, “you thought you were
going to ’ave some of that champagne, didn t
you ?” tSir ’Enry has a long memory.
